Review

## Classical and Emerging Characterization Techniques for Investigation of Ion Transport Mechanisms in Crystalline Fast Ionic Conductors

Yirong Gao, ∥ Adelaide M. Nolan, ∥ Peng Du, ∥ Yifan Wu, Chao Yang, Qianli Chen, * Yifei Mo, * and Shou-Hang Bo *

Cite This: Chem. Rev. 2020, 120, 5954 -6008

## ACCESS

Metrics &amp; More

Article Recommendations

ABSTRACT: Ion transport in crystalline fast ionic conductors is a complex physical phenomenon. Certain ionic species (e.g., Ag + , Cu + , Li + , F -, O 2 -, H + ) in a solid crystalline framework can move as fast as in liquids. This property, although only observed in a limited number of materials, is a key enabler for a broad range of technologies, including batteries, fuel cells, and sensors. However, the mechanisms of ion transport in the crystal lattice of fast ionic conductors are still not fully understood despite the substantial progress achieved in the last 40 years, partly because of the wide range of length and time scales involved in the complex migration processes of ions in solids. Without a comprehensive understanding of these ion transport mechanisms, the rational design of new fast ionic conductors is not possible. In this review, we cover classical and emerging characterization techniques (both experimental and computational) that can be used to investigate ion transport processes in bulk crystalline inorganic materials which exhibit predominant ion conduction (i.e., negligible electronic conductivity) with a primary focus on literature published after 2000 and critically assess their

strengths and limitations. Together with an overview of recent understanding, we highlight the need for a combined experimental and computational approach to study ion transport in solids of desired time and length scales and for precise measurements of physical parameters related to ion transport.

## 1. INTRODUCTION

Fast ionic conductors are conventionally de /uniFB01 ned as materials, at solid state, which exhibit ionic conductivity comparable to or even higher than that in liquid state (usually for aqueous solution). This property makes fast ionic conductors appealing for several advanced technologies, including solid-state batteries, fuel cells, and gas sensors. 1 -3 The discovery of lithium fast ionic conductors with conductivity higher than 10 mS cm -1 at room temperature has led to the development of solid-state batteries with improved rate capability (i.e., less time required to charge the battery) compared with that of conventional liquidelectrolyte-based lithium-ion batteries. 3 With improved ion transport properties, oxide-ion and proton conductors promote the realization of solid-state fuel cells with diversi /uniFB01 ed chemical fuels without the need for noble metal catalysts compared with the fuel cells based on polymer membranes. 2 Compared with liquids, fast ionic conductors are safer choices as they are not /uniFB02 ammable or corrosive. In addition, fast ionic conductors can operate in a wider temperature range with greater selectivity than their liquid counterparts. 4 -10

However, fast ionic conductors in working solid-state devices need to simultaneously satisfy a wide array of desired attributes, including high ionic conductivity, good chemical, mechanical, and thermal stability, good processability, high selectivity, and low cost. These requirements substantially narrow down the list of candidates that can be applied in real devices. For oxide-ion and proton conductors used in solid oxide fuel cells (SOFCs), to achieve the considerable ionic conductivity required for practical application, the operating temperature of SOFCs usually is as high as 700 ° C, which raises the manufacturing cost and demands extra energy input. Similarly, in the rapidly evolving /uniFB01 eld of solid-state batteries, no solid-state electrolytes employing fast lithium ionic conductors can yet satisfy all the required attributes. 11

To date, fast ionic conductors with various mobile ionic species, including but not limited to Li + , Na + , H + , Ag + , Cu + , F -, O 2 -, and H -, have been discovered and summarized in recent reviews. 4,10 -15 We note that both monovalent and multivalent ions, such as O 2 -and Mg 2+ , can be highly mobile in solids, with some of the recently discovered Mg-ion conductors exhibiting ionic conductivity as high as 0.1 mS cm -1 at room temperature. 7 Among all the ionic conductors, Li- and Na-ion conductors and proton and oxide-ion conductors have been the most heavily investigated since 2000, primarily because of their technological importance in batteries and fuel cells, respectively (Figure 1).

To facilitate comparison, the ionic conductivities of the best performing ionic conductors are summarized in Table 1. We note that conductivity is not simply correlated to ionic radii or charge per ionic radii, as classical electrostatic interaction

Figure 1. Publications on di /uniFB00 erent ionic conductors since 2000. Search on Web of Science for ' ion conductor ' , ' fast-ionic conductor ' , ' superionic conductor ' , ' superionic conductor ' , ' solid electrolyte ' , or ' solid-state electrolyte ' and ' X-ion or X ' (X = Li, Na, Mg, Ag, Cu, H, O, F) in the abstract, title, or subject /uniFB01 eld. For the publication statistics of 2019, data was only collected until October.

suggests. For instance, superionic conductivity at room temperature is usually observed for lithium- and sodium-ion conductors but not for proton conductors. The structure type also di /uniFB00 ers for the best performing ionic conductors of di /uniFB00 erent di /uniFB00 using species. Overall, these observations suggest that ion transport in solids is a complex process, which may vary across di /uniFB00 erent di /uniFB00 using species.

A comprehensive understanding of fast ionic conduction in crystalline solids can lead to generalized design rules for improved fast ionic conductors, but this understanding is challenging to attain. The classical picture of ion transport in crystalline solids is that ions jump between di /uniFB00 erent crystallographic sites, either one-by-one or collectively. Each jump with a distance of a few angstroms is associated with an activation barrier that is determined by the energy landscape created by the arrangement of atoms in the crystalline solid. This activation barrier directly a /uniFB00 ects the attempt frequency of ions (usually on the order of THz to MHz) and the successful jump frequencies that will result in macroscopic ion transport. However, the real picture of ion transport in solids is in fact far more complex. First, crystallographic disorder and local distortion are often present in crystalline solids at /uniFB01 nite temperatures. Therefore, ion transport is a /uniFB00 ected not only by the long-range bulk structure (e.g., tetragonal vs cubic garnet) but also by the structural features at local (angstroms) and intermediate (tens of angstroms) ranges. For instance, lithium transport in Li10GeP2S12 (LGPS) is a /uniFB00 ected by speci /uniFB01 c local con /uniFB01 gurations of Ge and P (Ge/P shares the same crystallographic site), where the activation barriers for lithium transport range from 60 to 420 meV depending on how Ge and P atoms arrange locally. 23 Local distortion (e.g., octahedral tilting in a perovskite) can also strongly a /uniFB00 ect the ionic transport process. 24 Indeed, recent computational works indicate that fast ionic conduction is linked with local geometric frustration of the Li sublattice. 25 -28 Second, ion transport in crystalline solids is a dynamic process that spans a wide range of time scales. Starting from the vibration of atoms (ps) to ion jumps between di /uniFB00 erent crystallographic sites (ps to ms), the time scale of microscopic ion transport events can cross several orders of magnitude. Finally, ion transport in solids is coupled with phonons and can be coupled with electrons (or holes). 29 -34 All these factors add additional complexity when considering ion transport processes in crystalline solids.

Table 1. Best Performing Ionic Conductors

The di /uniFB03 culties in understanding ion transport in crystalline solids and in further correlating the structures with ion transport properties are primarily 2-fold. First, defects and disorders in the crystalline structure are di /uniFB03 cult to characterize. When classical crystallography based on perfect periodicity is inadequate, tools and theories are needed to understand both the local and average structure of crystalline solids. This is particularly relevant for fast ionic conductors, as a majority of these conductors are rich in defects and disorder. Without understanding these defects and disorder in detail, correlation can hardly be made between structural and ion transport properties. Second, the development of a comprehensive physical picture of ion transport is di /uniFB03 cult. Ion transport crosses a wide range of time scales and involves structural relaxation at a wide range of length scales. Although most of these time and length scales can be probed today by the rapidly developing experimental and computational techniques, it remains challenging to capture the ion transport events occurring at certain time and length scales.

In Figure 2, we summarize the time and length scales accessible by existing experimental techniques. Although ion motion from 0.1 Hz (or 10 s) to 100 THz (or 10 -14 s) can be probed, no single technique can fully cover the ion transport processes at a broad range of time scale. 35 Similarly, it is impossible to capture structural features at various length scales using one single technique.

Inconsistencies also exist in literature which further complicates the understanding of ion transport mechanisms. For instance, di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cients can be estimated from molecular dynamics (MD) simulation, tracer di /uniFB00 usion measurements, conventional relaxometry measurements, and pulsed /uniFB01 eld gradient nuclear magnetic resonance (PFG NMR) measurements, to name a few approaches. However, even for the same material, the di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cients measured with di /uniFB00 erent techniques can still be drastically di /uniFB00 erent. In the lithium-ion conductor Li 10GeP2S12, di /uniFB00 erent di /uniFB00 usion coef/uniFB01 cients were obtained with di /uniFB00 erent techniques: 2.2 × 10 -12 m 2

s -1 using PFG NMR, 1.3 × 10 -8 m 2 s -1 using NMR relaxometry, 36 and 1.4 × 10 -11 m 2 s -1 from ab initio MD simulation. 37 -39 A comprehensive understanding of the physics behind each probing technique and the physical meaning behind each measured di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient is therefore needed. Besides, aided by large databases of well-computed properties of materials and high throughput methods of computing iontransport-related properties, 40,41 ' stable ' superionic conductors can be theoretically predicted. However, to better guide the experimental e /uniFB00 orts of syntheses, it is important to consider the limitations and assumptions of computational techniques used to assess stability and the di /uniFB00 erences between di /uniFB00 erent evaluation methods.

In this review, we critically assess the capabilities of existing and emerging characterization techniques that can be used to probe ion transport in solids, with a particular focus on recent advances reported after 2000. In the last two decades, structural information on a wide range of time and length scales has become accessible, and unprecedented details regarding disorder and local distortions in solids have been unraveled. By reviewing these activities, we hope to provide the community with a present-day picture of the available tools and the missing ' Legos ' to understand ion transport processes in solids. We focus on crystalline inorganic materials which exhibit predominant ion conduction with negligible electronic conductivity in this review because these materials are ideal model systems to reveal the fundamental correlation between structure and ion transport processes. In this regard, glass, amorphous, and polymeric materials that lack long-range order are much more challenging to characterize and are beyond the scope of this review.

The review is structured as follows. We /uniFB01 rst brie /uniFB02 y overview ion transport mechanisms and the physical parameters related to ion transport. Then we discuss the relation between these physical parameters in the ion transport process. This discussion is followed by case studies of classical techniques, both

experimental and computational, investigating ion transport processes in crystalline inorganic solids, with a particular focus on how the related physical parameters can be measured and how these physical parameters are linked to ion transport mechanisms. With this information in mind, we further discuss the de /uniFB01 nitions and measurements of a few important yet seemingly confusing physical parameters relevant to ion transport (i.e., activation barrier, mobile ion concentration, and di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient) and screening criteria of stable superionic conductors from computation. In the discussion section, we summarize a few cases where di /uniFB00 erent techniques are combined to yield relatively complete pictures of ion transport processes. Current di /uniFB03 culties and emerging techniques for probing ion transport processes are highlighted at the end.

## 2. CLASSICAL UNDERSTANDING OF ION TRANSPORT MECHANISMS

## 2.1. Fundamental Physics

For di /uniFB00 erent types of ionic conductors and ion transport mechanisms, various theoretical models have been put forward to describe the ion transport processes. 42,43 In all these models, a commonset of physical parameters exists that are relevant to ion transport and are central to understanding the mechanisms of ion transport in solids. Below, we /uniFB01 rst discuss these physical parameters in the context of uncorrelated ion transport mechanisms, where the ion-hopping events show no correlation with each other with respect to time or space in the materials. Wethen extend the discussion to correlated ion transport, and a few speci /uniFB01 c ion transport models proposed in the literatures.

The classical model describing ion transport in solids treats ion hops as uncorrelated and independent, which is often the key mechanism at the limit of dilute mobile ions. 44 Atomic hopping in solids depends on point defects, such as vacancies or interstitials, with /uniFB01 xed lengths of individual jumps. The individual ion hops with no correlation can be described by a random-walk model. 45 The random di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient D r , the di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient under the theoretical framework of random walk, can be expressed as

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

where Rn is the total displacement of a moving ion in n steps, t n is the duration to complete n steps of ion displacement, a is the hopping distance between two neighboring sites or free path, ν is the jump frequency of successful jumps, and b is a geometry factor of 2, 4, or 6 for one-, two-, or three-dimensional di /uniFB00 usion, respectively. Equation 1 is called the Einstein relation or the Einstein -Smoluchowski relation.

Comparing ion hopping to thermally activated Brownian motion, the jump frequency v can be further described by eq 2.

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

Here ν 0 is the attempt frequency, or the number of attempted jumps within unit time, and includes both successful jumps that can lead to macroscopic di /uniFB00 usion and unsuccessful ones. Δ G is the Gibbs free energy of activation, k B is the Boltzmann constant, T is the absolute temperature in Kelvin, Δ S is the activation entropy, and Δ H is the activation enthalpy (this is also called the activation energy or activation barrier and denoted E a ). Substituting ν in eq 1 with that in eq 2, we obtain

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

The physical meaning of the activation enthalpy Δ H depends on the nature of defects. Intrinsic defects, including vacancies and self-interstitials, are thermally activated. Therefore, the activation energy of ion transport processes that involve such defects is the sum of the defect formation energy and migration energy. Extrinsic defects are usually introduced by doping. In general, the concentration of extrinsic defects far exceeds that of thermally activated intrinsic defects. The activation energy under such conditions can usually be approximated as the migration energy if there is no trapping between the mobile ion and dopants or other defects. 46,47 The ionic conductivity determined by extrinsic defects has been widely studied in lithium super ionic conductors (LISICONs), 48,49 sodium super ionic conductors (NASICONs), 50 and garnets. 51,52 Proton conduction in perovskite materials also arises from the generation of extrinsic defects upon water incorporation. 53 In complicated scenarios in which the interaction between defects (either intrinsic or extrinsic) and mobile ions is strong (e.g., in proton or oxide-ion conductors), an additional term of the defect trapping energy (similar to the defect formation energy) must be included. 46,54 -56

The random di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient D r is de /uniFB01 ned from the microscopic picture of uncorrelated random ion jumps at the dilute concentration limit. D r can be related to the macroscopic long-range di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient D σ through the tracer di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient D * , Haven ratio H R and correlation factor f . D σ is linked to the ionic conductivity σ through the Nernst -Einstein relation:

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

where c is the mobile ion concentration and q is the charge of mobile ion. From eq 4, which describes a macroscopically observable quantity (i.e., D σ or σ ), microscopic physical parameters (e.g., the jump distance and frequency) can be derived. We discuss the de /uniFB01 nitions of the tracer di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient, Haven ratio, and correlation factor, as well as their relationship to the random di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient D r in the following paragraph.

The tracer di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient D * is often used to describe ion transport at relatively large length scales. 57 The classical approach to measure the tracer di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient is to induce a radioactive tracer that is chemically identical to the mobile species investigated (e.g., deuterium in the case of proton conductors). By measuring the concentration depth pro /uniFB01 le within the material after the radioactive tracer is introduced for a certain time, D * can be obtained by /uniFB01 tting according to Fick ' s law. The correlation factor f is de /uniFB01 ned as the ratio between the tracer di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient D * and the random di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient D r as f = D * / D r . 45,58 This correlation factor is also named as tracer correlation factor and corresponds to single-ion correlations. 59 The Haven ratio relates the long-range di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient D σ to the tracer di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient D * of an ionic conductor as H R = D * / D σ , 60 and describes the strength of multiion correlations. Overall, = = σ D D f D f H I r r R . The factor f I here denotes the collective correlation factor, which measures the strength of distinct-ion correlation e /uniFB00 ects, analogous to vacancywind e /uniFB00 ects. 59,61 Wenote that the tracer di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient D *

can be precisely determined for certain classes of ionic conductors but D σ cannot. D σ is a derived quantity and is not directly measured due to the ambiguity in the de /uniFB01 nition of c . The random di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient D r can be determined by QENS if the proper model is applied, whereas for other techniques such as NMR, the calculation of D r needs the assumption that jump distance a is the distance between two atoms in the crystal structure. These assumptions also cause ambiguity in the evaluation of f and H R , posing a fundamental challenge to quantify various aspects of the ion transport processes for indepth understanding.

where the factor of 1 /6 results from the assumed 3D isotropic motion as in the random walk model (i.e., b = 6). In random and uncorrelated ion transport processes, where the correlation factor f and Haven ration H R are both 1, the above eq 5 can be simpli /uniFB01 ed as

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

Both the correlation factor f and Haven ratio H R serve as indicators of the correlated e /uniFB00 ect in ion transport processes. Correlated ion transport is a multiparticle di /uniFB00 usion phenomenon. Individual hops in this case are no longer independent but involve correlation e /uniFB00 ects (mobile ion -mobile ion, and/or mobile ion -skeleton) which deviate from random walks. For such processes, the correlation factor f and Haven ratio H R take nonunity values.

The correlation factor f usually takes a value between 0 and 1. 62 -67 Various factors including the mobile ion concentration, geometric structure, and di /uniFB00 usion mechanism can a /uniFB00 ect the correlation factor. Table 2 summarizes the values of the correlation factors derived by calculations for ion transport processes in di /uniFB00 erent systems, from which the dependence of the correlation factors on dimensionality, lattice type, and di /uniFB00 usion mechanism can be observed. Most of the results were obtained by matrix method and Monte Carlo method. We further take an extreme condition of the vacancy mechanism in a one-dimensional atom chain here as an example to illustrate the correlation factor (Figure 3). For a moving atom (or tracer, shown as the red circle in Figure 3) with Z neighboring sites, when the moving atom jumps to one of the neighboring sites ( 1 / Z probability), a vacancy -tracer exchange process is completed. For a 1D chain, Z equals to two. The new vacancy position hence allows for a reverse jump, canceling another forward one, leading to -( ) n 1 2 Z 1 e /uniFB00 ective jumps out of n . 66 As illustrated in Figure 3, the tracer atom can only jump to and from the two neighboring sites, resulting in a long-range tracer di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient of zero and hence a correlation factor f = 0.

As for the Haven ration H R, a straightforward physical meaning of Haven ratio remains absent 45 except in certain models (e.g., uncorrelated vacancy, correlated interstitialcy). A /uniFB00 ected by various factors, H R takes values from 0 to 1 in most ionic conductors with negligible electronic conductivity but can

Various di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cients have been discussed above, which is to some extent rather confusing. Here, we reiterate and outline the de /uniFB01 nitions and measurement techniques of di /uniFB00 erent di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cients. The measurement techniques will also be discussed in detail in section 3 and section 4.3. In general, di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cients can be categorized as microscopic or macroscopic. The microscopic di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cients can be probed using microscopic techniques, such as variable-temperature NMR, NMR relaxometry, 2D NMR, μ + SR, and QENS. These di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cients are random di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cients D r and can be described by the Einstein -Smoluchowski relationship (eq 1), which relates D r to the jump distance and residence time of an atom on a certain lattice position. Tracer di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient D * is one of the macroscopic di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cients that describes ion transport at relatively large length scales. D * is measured by detecting isotopic tracers, hence the name, which is usually probed using tracer di /uniFB00 usion or the pulsed /uniFB01 eld gradient NMR technique. The long-range di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient D σ is another macroscopic di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient measured by electrochemical impedance spectroscopy that relates ionic conductivity with di /uniFB00 usivity by the Nernst -Einstein eq (eq 4).

Below, we discuss a few speci /uniFB01 c correlated ion transport models. These ion transport processes are very complex, and as a result, most have not been quantitatively analyzed under the context of eq 5. However, we note that some of the correlated

2.1.1. Jump Relaxation Model. Correlated forward -backward hopping is a feature that is evident in correlated ion transport processes. A physical explanation was provided in the jump relaxation model, which accounts for the interactions among the mobile ions (i.e., the Coulomb-cage e /uniFB00 ect) and between the mobile and immobile ions (i.e., periodic lattice potential). 81 In a crystalline solid electrolyte, mobile ions experience a repulsion interaction because of neighboring samecharge mobile ions, which changes during ion migration. The average charge of these surrounding mobile ions is approxi- where b denotes the dimensionality factor, N represents the number of mobile ions, and M represents the total number of steps that the mobile ions traveled. The total displacement ⟨ Rn 2 ⟩ as shown in eq 1 is replaced by an averaged sum (by 2 N ) of total displacement of N mobile ions in M steps. Besides, tracer di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient D * , ionic conductivity σ , long-range di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient D σ , correlation factor f , Haven ratio H R , and collective correlation factor f I can all be computed using MD. 59,80

mated as a cloud of ions, and the moving ion under consideration is viewed as being situated in a Coulomb cage produced by the ion cloud (Figure 4a). The Coulomb-cage e /uniFB00 ect generates a local potential minimum for each mobile ion such that each moving ion is far enough from the others, producing a set of equilibrium positions in space. Another crucial interaction is between mobile and immobile ions. Because of the periodicity of a crystal, the interaction potential for such an interaction is also periodic in space. Superposition of the local Coulomb-cage-e /uniFB00 ect minimum and long-range periodic lattice potential thus yields the overall potential well experienced by the moving ion (Figure 4b). When ion migration occurs, e.g., the ion jumps from lattice site A to B (Figure 4b), the local potential minimum for each moving ion as resulting from the Coulomb-cage e /uniFB00 ect is perturbed while the periodic lattice potential superimposed on the moving ion remains approximately unaltered. This drives the system away from equilibrium. Thus, a structural relaxation process must occur such that the equilibrium is restored. Two competing relaxation processes are possible (Figure 4b). First, as a many-particle route, when the ion hops to site B, the ion cloud also moves forward, establishing a new local Coulomb-cage-e /uniFB00 ect minimum and resulting in a new equilibrium and a net ionic current. Second, as a single-particle route, the ion may also hop back to A from B, restoring the equilibrium with no detectable ionic current. A comparison is shown in the lower part of Figure 4c. Hopping forward, the mobile ion must overcome an energy barrier of δ B → C( t ) as denoted in Figure 4c. Hopping back, the

Figure 5. Illustration of (a) interstitialcy (or knock-o /uniFB00 ) mechanism in the O-ion conductor La2CoO4+ δ , where ion transport proceeds by interstitial O atoms hopping to occupied sites and ' knocking-o /uniFB00 ' previously existing ones (A -B -C) in lieu of an interstitial process (A -A ′ ), as revealed by DFT computations and MD simulations. The bottom panel shows the structure of La2CoO4+ δ viewed along c and b axes, where red circles represent lattice oxygen sites and empty circles stand for interstitial sites. Reproduced with permission from ref 90. Copyright 2011 the PCCP Owner Societies. (b) Illustration of mechanism by which ion migration barrier is lowered through a multi-ion concerted migration. Empty circles represent vacant sites for ion transport, while the red circles stand for mobile ion(s) that migrate during single- or multi-ion migration. Reproduced with permission from ref 80. Copyright 2017 Springer Nature.

energy barrier is δ B → A ( t ), which is much smaller than δ B → C( t ), as determined by the overall energy landscape. Consequently, there is usually a strong preference for backward hopping in correlated ion transport processes, which could be the origin of the smaller-than-one correlation factors f for such processes. In other words, as explained by the jump relaxation model, because of correlation e /uniFB00 ects, not all elementary hops contribute to macroscopic long-range ion migration.

Through the basic assumptions of the jump relaxation model, one can relate ion transport processes to the dielectric spectra (or conductivity spectra) of a material, although inconsistencies exist between theoretical modeling and experiments in particular at extreme long-time or low-frequency. Later, to improve the accuracy of theoretical models, the jump relaxation model was re /uniFB01 ned with the proposed concepts of mismatch and relaxation (CMR) 82 and mismatch generated relaxation for the accommodation and transport of ions (MIGRATION). 83,84 In the re /uniFB01 ned model, the relaxation rates of single- and many-particle routes are assumed to be proportional to each other throughout the ion migration process, both decaying exponentially with time. In addition, the ion cloud constituted by the nearest neighbors is assumed to always exert an electrostatic repulsion on the mobile ion. This model has achieved great success in describing ion transport processes in glassy and disordered crystalline materials. However, the Haven ratio is taken as unity in such models, which require future improvement.

2.1.2. Concerted Migration. Concerted migration, 85 -89 interstitialcy, 90 -94 and ' knock-o /uniFB00 ' (also called ' knockon ' ) 23,95,96 mechanisms are similar in nature, where two or more mobile ions simultaneously jump to occupied neighboring sites. The interstitialcy mechanism has been mostly reported for oxide-ion conductors. A similar mechanism was also proposed for Li-ion conductors, although with di /uniFB00 erent nomenclatures such as ' knock-o /uniFB00 ' , ' knock-on ' , or ' kick-out ' mechanism. Similar to the jump relaxation model, electrostatic repulsion between the migrating and neighboring mobile ions is considered, which triggers simultaneous and continuous jumping of multiple ions. 23,90,96 -104 Schematic illustrations of the interstitialcy and ' knock-o /uniFB00 ' mechanisms are provided in parts a and b of Figure 5, respectively. 90 On the basis of MD simulations, the lowest-energy di /uniFB00 usion pathway in the oxideion conductor La2CoO4+ δ was revealed to be A -B -C, in which interstitial O atoms (A) hop toward occupied sites B and ' knock-o /uniFB00 ' the B-site O atom, which jumps to the next interstitial site C. For concerted migration, Coulomb interaction among mobile ions can lead to lower migration barriers than those of independent, uncorrelated hops. 37,80,87 The concerted migration barrier, caused by the e /uniFB00 ect of Coulombic repulsion, has been quanti /uniFB01 ed by ab initio molecular dynamics (AIMD) simulations in Li-ion conductor Li10GeP2S12 (LGPS), cubic Li7La3Zr2O12 ( c -LLZO), and Li1.3Al0.3 Ti1.7(PO4)3 (LATP). 80 When the Li concentration is high, a fraction of Li ions must occupy the crystallographic sites that have higher energies in addition to other lithium ions at low-energy sites and can be equilibrated at these high-energy sites, as a result of strong Coulomb repulsion from other Li ions. 80 In macroscopic ion transport, the migration of lithium ions at high- and low-energy sites is synchronized. In such a concerted migration of multiple ions, lithium ions at high-energy sites would move downhill in the energy landscape and partially cancel out the required energy

Figure 6. (a) Illustration of paddle-wheel mechanism in Na2SO4, in which Na + hopping is triggered by rotational motion of the SO 4 2 -group. Reproduced with permission from ref 106. for lithium ions at low-energy sites climbing uphill, resulting in low-barrier concerted migration, 80 as illustrated in Figure 5c.

2.1.3. Ion Migration Facilitated by Polyanion Reorientation. The jump relaxation model treats the structural lattice as mobile and immobile ions. However, immobile ions without translational motion can still have degrees of freedom for rotation, which correlates with the migration of moving ions. We thus discuss anion reorientation and its correlation with ion transport in this section. The paddle wheel e /uniFB00 ect was /uniFB01 rst proposed by Kvist. et al. 105 for Li2SO4, followed by works on substituted Na3PO4, i.e., Na3PO4 -Na2SO4 solid solution, 43 antiperovskite variants (NO2)ONa3, 43 closo-borate, and others. 106 -113 The migration barrier for sodium is lowered by the rotation of SO 4 tetrahedra, which propels sodium ions, as illustrated in Figure 6a. 106 Such a mechanism is commonly observed in plastic and rotator phases, i.e., orientationally disordered phases (ODPs). Polyanions contributing to this mechanism include nitrites, nitrates, carbonates, (hydro)sulfates, and (hydro)phosphates. 114 -116 The revolving door mechanism is similar to the paddle wheel mechanism involving both anionic groups and cations; however, instead of being propelled by anion reorientation, cation movement is suggested to induce anion reorientation. 117 Anion reorientation has also been reported in metal borohydrides, alkali tri /uniFB02 uoromethanesulfonates MCF3SO3 (M = Li, Na), 118 and antiperovskite ionic conductor with complex anions. 9,119 Most metal borohydrides with BH4 -, B10H10 2 -, B12H12 2 -, CB9H10 -, and CB11H12 -groups undergo anion reorientation processes. 120 -130 A schematic illustration of the anion reorientation mechanism is provided in the work of Lu et al., 128 reproduced in Figure 7. The correlation between Li + /Na + cation hopping and borohydride anion rotation is indicated by the simultaneous increase of the proton

spin -lattice relaxation rate and decrease of the 7 Li NMR line width with varying temperatures.

In ion conductors of anions (such as oxide-ion conductors), anion migration can also be coupled with the rotation of polyanions. The cog-wheel mechanism is described as processes of dynamic orientational correlations, in which the bonding environments of the polyanions are dynamically switching between two related con /uniFB01 gurations (e.g., GaO 4 and O3Ga-OGaO3) during ion transport. 131 -133 From density functional theory (DFT) calculations, oxygen migration within the La1 -x Ba1+ x GaO4 -x /2 crystal structure was shown to proceed by reorientation of polyanion groups (GaO 4 ) and the exchange of oxygen atoms, i.e., breaking and reforming of Ga 2 O7 units. 133 A similar mechanism was also proposed in oxygen conductors Bi 1 -x Sr x VO4 -0.5 x , 134 Bi 1 -x V x O1.5+ x , 135,136 and Bi26Mo10O69, 137 in which anion groups all undergo breaking, rotation, and reformation to facilitate oxygen transport in the structures. A schematic illustration of ion transport mechanism of Bi26Mo10O69 is presented in Figure 6c.

In most cases discussed above, ion migration and anion reorientation are observed to occur simultaneously. However, questions still remain as to whether ion migration and anion reorientation are casually related, and if yes, whether ion migration causes anion reorientation or whether the reverse is true.

2.1.4. Phonon-E /uniFB00 ect Migration. In addition to cation -cation repulsion and anion-reorientation-facilitated ion migration, low-energy phonon mode (or lattice vibration) was also considered to trigger ion migration. In the Brownmillerite structure of oxide-ion conductor SrFeO2.5 with a 1D oxygen vacancy channel along [110], oxygen di /uniFB00 usion proceeds with the help of a ' zigzag to zagzig ' phonon con /uniFB01 guration switch at the picosecond scale between the T1 and T2 con /uniFB01 gurations, as illustrated in Figure 8. 138 The T1 and T2 con /uniFB01 gurations are indicated in the T 3 con /uniFB01 guration as gray- and black-bordered triangles, respectively. With certain switching frequency, the T 1 and T2 con /uniFB01 gurations exchange constantly, behaving like a ' gate ' in the 1D di /uniFB00 usion channel of oxygen, as described by the

Figure 8. Schematics illustrations of (a) crystal structure of SrFeO 2.5 , and (b) O atom migration in a phonon-triggered ' zigzag -zagzig ' mechanism based on continuous con /uniFB01 guration switch between T 1 and T2. T3 provides a comparison between T1 and T2 con /uniFB01 gurations. Red dots represent O sites occupied during ' zigzag-zagzig ' ion transport. Reproduced with permission from ref 138. authors. Speci /uniFB01 c low-energy (7 meV) lattice modes enables movement of apical oxygen atoms of FeO6 octahedra, which is proposed based on inelastic neutron scattering (INS) results along with DFT computations. This mechanism enables the use of new approaches for designing e /uniFB03 cient oxide-ion conductors, observed in other Ruddlesden-Popper-type oxide-ion conductors, e.g., Nd2NiO4+ δ , 139 La2CuO4+ δ . 140 A more complicated ion transport mechanism was revealed by Chroneos et al. for La2NiO4+ δ 92 and by Li et al. 141 for Ln2NiO4+ δ , where phonon modes and the interstitialcy mechanism were observed to be coupled to assist oxygen transport, based on phonon mode softness obtained by frozen phonons and density functional perturbation theory (DFPT) and di /uniFB00 usion paths revealed by the climbing image-nudged elastic band (CI-NEB) method.

## 2.2. Structural Factors Relevant to Ion Transport

According to eq 5, mobile ion concentration c , mobile ion charge q , jump distance a , jump frequency ν 0 , temperature T , correlation factor f , Haven ratio H R , migration entropy Δ S , and activation energy Δ H can all a /uniFB00 ect the ionic conductivity. Here, we extend our discussion to structural factors that a /uniFB00 ect these parameters and hence a /uniFB00 ect the ionic conductivity. A schematic illustration showing relationships among these structural factors, ion-transport-related physical parameters is presented in Figure 9. Ion correlations (i.e., f and H R ) were not

Figure 9. Relationships among the ionic conductivity and structure factors and ion-transport-related physical parameters. All parameters in /uniFB01 gure can a /uniFB00 ect ionic conductivity in di /uniFB00 erent ways. Parameters in the outermost circle are structural factors, such as bottleneck, lattice volume, lattice dynamics, local disorder, local distortion, defect, and coordination, which in /uniFB02 uence the parameter within the same sector and then a /uniFB00 ect the ionic conductivity. Parameters directly a /uniFB00 ect the ionic conductivity are shown in the inner circle with black border, including activation energy, jump distance, mobile ion concentration, attempt frequency, and entropy.

included in the /uniFB01 gure because the impact of structural parameters on ion correlations is not clear, although ion correlations certainly a /uniFB00 ect ionic conductivity. We /uniFB01 rst note that the physical parameters relevant to ion transport are mainly a /uniFB00 ected by the lattice volume, bottleneck size for ion transport, coordination, local disorder, local distortion, defects, and lattice dynamics. 142 Second, several of these structural factors (e.g., the lattice volume) a /uniFB00 ect multiple physical parameters relevant to ion transport and further a /uniFB00 ect ionic conductivity in a very complex way. Each structural factor and its e /uniFB00 ect on iontransport-relevant physical parameters are discussed as follows.

2.2.1. Lattice Volume and Bottleneck. For solid-state ionic conductors, ions always move through empty space. In principle, for the same structure, a larger lattice volume means larger empty space in the crystal structure, which presumably

results in a reduced activation barrier and increased jump distance, thus favoring facile ion transport. 13 In practice, however, this may not always be the case. For example, lattice volume also a /uniFB00 ect the site energy of the ions 142 -146 and a /uniFB00 ect the potential energy surface in the crystal structures in a complex way.

The lattice volume can be tuned through either mechanical strain or chemical substitution. Mechanical strain, being either tensile or compressive, can be used to study the e /uniFB00 ect of lattice volume on ion transport or to tune the ionic conductivity. 147 -152 AIMD simulation of LGPS 153 demonstrated that isotropically compressing the crystal structure (smaller lattice volume) leads to a greatly increased activation barrier and decreased ionic conductivity. In addition to the activation barrier, mechanical strain can also alter the attempt frequency of moving ions. For example, the Ce -Ostretching modes for the proton conductor BaCe0.8 Y0.2O3 -δ was observed to become harder with increasing pressure, 154 which directly a /uniFB00 ects the activation barrier and attempt frequency. Chemical substitution is another approach that is extensively used to change the lattice volume. 155 -157 However, chemical substitution simultaneously introduces changes to the chemical environment, such as local defect, coordination, energy landscape, and site disordering. Therefore, chemical substitution is not suggested as a clear-cut method to study the correlation between lattice volume and ion transport properties.

The bottleneck size of a structure very often scales with the lattice volume and thus can a /uniFB00 ect the ion transport process in a similar way as the lattice volume. 13,158 However, exceptions do exist. For Li10MP2S12 (M = Ge, Sn), when changing Ge 4+ to Sn 4+ , the lattice volume increases because of the larger ionic size of Sn 4+ , but the bottleneck along the c -axis is tightened due to the weak interaction between Sn 4+ and S 2 -, thus hindering the Li ionic transport. 33

2.2.2. Coordination. The coordination environments of ions moving along the migration path primarily a /uniFB00 ect the activation barrier of ion transport but can also a /uniFB00 ect the jump distance. Previous computational works 144,145 suggest that the migration energy of ions in the crystalline lattices can be rationalized by the changes of anion coordination environment along the migration path. The ion migration energy of lithium ion in spinel is larger than in olivine and layered structures because lithium occupies tetrahedral site in spinels (preferred coordination for Li + ), while lithium ions in the other two structures are six-coordinated. Similar phenomenon was also observed in divalent ionic conductors. In spinels, the migration energy of Zn 2+ is larger than those of Mg 2+ and Ca 2+ because Zn 2+ prefers tetrahedral coordination but Ca 2+ and Mg 2+ do not. In a recent work, high magnesium-ion mobility was experimentally observed in spinel MgSc2Se4, in which Mg atoms reside at the unfavorable tetrahedral sites but pass through an intermediate state with a more favorable octahedral coordination. This process leads to a smaller di /uniFB00 erence of the site energy between the start and intermediate states and thus /uniFB02 attening of the energy landscape along the migration path. 7

In addition, the anion lattice structure greatly a /uniFB00 ects cation transport. 143 Ion conductors with less densely packed bodycentered cubic (bcc) anion sublattice, in which lithium ions move directly from one tetrahedral site to another, generally have a low activation barrier and high ionic conductivity.

2.2.3. Local Disorder and Distortion. Most superionic conductors exhibit a wide range of local disorder in lattice and distortion in structures. The disordering can occur between cations and anions or mobile and immobile ions. Distortion generally means deviation of the bond distances and/or angles from the perfect symmetry and periodicity suggested by classical crystallography.

It is important to note that local disorder of mobile ion sublattice and distortion of the local crystal structure often appear together and are thus highly correlated. In a recently discovered lithium-ion conductor LiTi 2(PS4)3 (LTPS), Hautier et al. demonstrated that Li ions do not stay on well-de /uniFB01 ned crystallographic sites but rather sit in the locally distorted crystallographic space, leading to a frustrated energy landscape for fast Li-ion di /uniFB00 usion. 26 He et al. performed topological analyses on the crystal structures of all Li-containing oxides and sul /uniFB01 des and found such locally enlarged or distorted crystallography coexists with local Li-site disordering. 142 For the perovskite structure Li 3 x La2/3 -x □ 1/3 -2 x TiO3 ( □ refers to a vacancy), the lithium ion conductivity reaches a maximum at x = 0.1. 24 The maximized ionic conductivity at this composition is correlated to the arrangement of the cations (La 3+ and Li + ) and vacancies at A sites (Wycko /uniFB00 3 c ) and local distortions of the TaO6 octahedra caused by smaller lithium ions occupying the La 3+ site.

Many ionic conductors undergo an order -disorder superionic transition with sharply increased ionic conductivity and increased degree of disordering at high temperature. For example, the cubic lithium garnet (high-temperature phase) shows much higher ionic conductivity of 0.4 mS cm -1 at room temperature, compared with tetragonal garnet lithium conductors (low-temperature phase). This is attributed to the disordering of lithium in the tetrahedral and octahedral sites in the cubic structure, which are ordered in the tetragonal phase. 159 -161 The distortion of ZrO 6 octahedra is found to be correlated with the change of lithium occupancy from octahedral sites in the tetragonal phase to o /uniFB00 -centered octahedral sites in the cubic phase, which contributes to increased ionic conductivity. 159 Order -disorder phase transitions resulting in increased ionic conductivity were also observed in a number of sodium-ion and proton conductors. 122,125,162,163

In addition, the disordering of immobile cations or anions also greatly a /uniFB00 ects the mobile-ion migration. In lithium antiperovskites, locally interchanging one pair of Cl -O in disordered Li 3 OCl, i.e., swapping the A and B sites in the perovskite, leads to a distorted local structure and di /uniFB00 erent Li-ion migration barrier. As a result, a larger mean square displacement (MSD, see section 4.3) of lithium ions is observed compared with Li3OCl without O -Cl swapping. 164 In Li3OCl1 -x Br x antiperovskite lithium ion conductors, the activation barrier is sensitive to the arrangements of Cl and Br, of which the con /uniFB01 guration of a Cl-rich migration tunnel with Br-rich end points results in the lowest migration barrier among all investigated con /uniFB01 gurations. 165 In the oxide-ion conductor Na 0.5 Bi 0.5 TiO3 with a disordered A-site Na + /Bi 3+ cation sublattice, the local con /uniFB01 gurations of Na and Bi greatly a /uniFB00 ect the oxygen ion migration barriers, ranging from 0.22 eV to more than 2.0 eV for the pathways with local Bi -Bi coordination and Na -Na coordination, respectively. 79 Their AIMD simulations also predict that a completely ordered A-site Na + /Bi 3+ cation sublattice decreases the activation energy from 0.38 to 0.24 eV and increases oxygen-ion conductivity by 5-fold at 900 K, although the ordered A-site sublattice has a higher energy in this perovskite structure.

2.2.4. Defects. As previously discussed, defects in materials can be classi /uniFB01 ed as intrinsic or extrinsic, and the type of defect and their interactions can greatly a /uniFB00 ect the defect formation,

Figure 10. (a) Motional narrowing of the 7 Li NMRline width of polycrystalline Li 7PSe6. The /uniFB01 lled symbols refers to data recorded by a standard probe, box points is obtained by a cryo probe. Reproduced with permission from ref 176. Copyright 2013 PCCP Owner Societies. (b) 7 Li NMR SLR rates of garnet-type Li7La3Zr2O12 recorded in the laboratory ( ● ) and the rotating frame of reference ( ○ ). The solid lines represent /uniFB01 ts using the Lorentzianshaped spectral density functions (except the data points marked with a dot). migration path of moving ions, and ion migration enthalpy. Point defects such as vacancies 166 and interstitials 167 are often directly related to ion transport in crystalline conductors, and ion transport mechanisms in uncorrelated hopping are classi /uniFB01 ed as vacancy or interstitial transport mechanisms, according to the type of defects present in the material. Di /uniFB00 erent defect types lead to di /uniFB00 erent jump distances. Defect formation enthalpy is crucial to ionic transport. We devoted a subsection to discuss defect formation in section 4.1.

In addition, defect concentration is a /uniFB00 ected by the defect type, resulting in di /uniFB00 erent mobile ion concentration, which has a great e /uniFB00 ect on the ionic conductivity and activation energy. However, it should be noted that the defect concentration and mobile ion concentration are not equal in many ion-conducting systems.

2.2.5. Lattice Dynamics. Lattice dynamics describe the atomic vibrations within a crystal structure. The vibration frequency of ions at the lattice sites lies in the THz range (approximately 10 12 -10 13 Hz). The pioneering work of Wakamura has revealed that low activation energies of Li, Na, Ag, and Cu ionic conductors are associated with low-energy optical phonon. 168 However, the e /uniFB00 ect of lattice dynamics on the ion transport behavior has been less explored than the other factors discussed above. In recent works, 29 -32,169 the correlation between lattice dynamics and ion transport has been investigated from the following perspectives, making an important leap forward. We present a brief overview below and a detailed discussion in section 3.2.4.

Ionic conductivity and other related transport characteristics were studied under the context of phonon density of states (pDOS). It has been suggested that lower lithium vibration frequency or lower lithium phonon band center is correlated to a lower activation energy of fast ionic conductors, 29 which can be used as a descriptor to screen new lithium ion conductors. 169 Ionic transport was also analyzed with respect to the polarizability of the lattice and its corresponding acoustic phonon properties. 138 -141 Speci /uniFB01 cally, it has been demonstrated that the polarizability of the host ion lattice a /uniFB00 ects the lattice vibration strength as well as the activation barrier and prefactor. 170 -172

## 3. EXPERIMENTAL TECHNIQUES

In this section, we critically review existing experimental techniques used to study ion transport processes in crystalline inorganic materials. In section 3.1, techniques used to directly probe ion transport properties (e.g., activation barrier, di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient, and ionic conductivity) are /uniFB01 rst discussed. Then in section 3.2, we discuss structural probes (bulk and local structures and lattice dynamics), which are used to correlate structural factors with ion transport properties. Finally, in section 3.3, we compare the strengths and limitations of the di /uniFB00 erent experimental techniques. In each subsection, we show detailed examples with a primary focus on lithium, sodium, proton, and oxide-ion conductors, from which most of the reports were found after 2000 as indicated in Figure 1, but other types of ionic conductors are also covered.

## 3.1. Ion Transport

Ion transport in a crystal lattice involves multiple length scales which can be divided into microscopic and macroscopic levels. Macroscopic ion transport processes are produced via a series of microscopic ion transport events in di /uniFB00 erent time and length scales. Experimental techniques to probe microscopic and macroscopic ion transport processes are discussed below.

3.1.1. Microscopic Ion Transport. The nature of microscopic ion transport is the basis for understanding long-range ion transport processes. For various microscopic physical parameters that can be measured (e.g., the jump frequency, jump distance, random di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient D r , and activation energy), we should be fully aware of the time scale to which the probing experimental techniques are sensitive. For instance, a technique that is sensitive to the jump process between crystallographic sites, which occurs on the order of microseconds or even slower, cannot be applied to study the vibration processes at the picosecond scale. Microscopic ion dynamics are commonly studied by nuclear magnetic resonance, quasi-elastic neutron scattering, and muon spin relaxation. In the following, we will discuss how these techniques are used to study microscopic ion transport. In addition, we highlight the critical need for the use of complementary experimental techniques to study microscopic ion transport processes that cover a broad range of time scales.

3.1.1.1. Variable-Temperature Nuclear Magnetic Resonance. Solid-state nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) spectroscopy has been proven to be a versatile tool to study ion dynamics and is sensitive to a broad range of time scales (10 -10 to 10 2 s). 57 The mobile ion (e.g., Li + ) can be directly probed by NMR 173 -175 and then jump frequency, jump distance, di /uniFB00 usion

coe /uniFB03 cient, di /uniFB00 usion path topology, activation energy, and correlation e /uniFB00 ects during ion transport can be evaluated, provided that ion transport events fall within the time scales of NMR spectroscopy.

The line shape analysis of variable-temperature NMR spectra can be used to study the ion jump frequency, random di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient, and the associated activation barrier. At low temperature, the NMR central transition is broad and Gaussian-shaped, caused by various NMR interactions in solids, which is in stark contrast to the sharp lines observed in liquid NMRwhere rapid isotropic tumbling of molecules is present. At high temperature, as the ion jumps exceed the time scale of the relevant NMR interactions determined by the rigid-lattice line width, a sharp Lorentzian-shaped line with substantially reduced line width appears. This is re /uniFB02 ected in typical motional narrowing (MN) curve 176 (Figure 10a). At the onset temperature T onset of the ion motion, the jump frequency can be estimated by τ = -M 1 2rigid lattice . 177,178 The M 2rigid lattice is the second moment in the rigid lattice line and can be calculated based on the van Vleck expression 179 and is shown in eq 8: 180 -182

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

where, γ i is the gyromagnetic ratios of atoms, h is Planck ' s constant, I and S are the nuclear spin of their nuclear, and r jk is distances between interacting nuclei. It can be clearly seen that the total second moment measured for the resonant spin I consists of the second moment derived from interactions with resonant spins and the total interactions of resonant spins I with di /uniFB00 erent nonresonant spins S , which means that the average local magnetic /uniFB01 eld felt by the resonance spin is the sum of the magnetic /uniFB01 elds generated by all the resonant and nonresonant nuclei. For example, the Li jump frequencies at T onset for Li 10 GeP2S12, Li 11 SiP2 S12 , and Li 10SnP2S12 calculated by eq 8 are 1.4 × 10 -4 s -1 at 125 K, 1.5 × 10 -4 s -1 at 145 K, and 1.5 × 10 -4 s -1 at 145 K, respectively. Another method to calculate ion jump frequency using MN curve is through τ -1 ≈ 2 π × δ rl s -1 , where δ rl is the line width of the rigid lattice. 183 Wenote that this obtained jump frequency corresponds to that at this speci /uniFB01 c in /uniFB02 ection temperature T infle (Figure 10a), which di /uniFB00 ers at other temperatures. Moreover, the activation energy of the mobile ions can also be estimated using the empirical formula proposed by Waugh and Fedin, 184 E a(meV) ≈ 1.617 × T onset .

With the jump frequency in hand, one can further estimate the random di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient D r based on eq 1, assuming threedimensional di /uniFB00 usion with geometry factor b as 6. Given these conditions, eq 1 can be rewritten as eq 9:

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

here, a is the jump distance, which is generally considered as the distance between two sites, and τ is the jump time, i.e., the reciprocal of the jump frequency ν . We note that in NMR measurement, due to the absence of any chemical or electrical /uniFB01 eld gradient, ions move randomly in space without external driving force similar to the random walk model. Therefore, the di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient measured from these NMR techniques is the random walk di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient D r . Therefore, the di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient obtained in this way is microscopic in nature and can only provide information for a motion process at the time scale comparable to the relevant NMR interactions (e.g., the kHz -MHz scale for dipolar and quadrupolar interactions). In addition, the factor 1 /6 comes from the assumed 3D isotropic motion, which can di /uniFB00 er for correlated transport and motion processes with reduced dimensionality.

3.1.1.2. Nuclear Magnetic Resonance Relaxometry. Nuclear magnetic resonance relaxometry (NMR relaxometry) is another important method to probe microscopic ion transport processes and is more accurate than the MN curve analysis. Two NMR relaxation processes are relevant here: longitudinal relaxation (spin -lattice relaxation, SLR) and transverse relaxation (spin -spin relaxation, SSR). These two relaxation processes are sensitive to ion transport processes at di /uniFB00 erent time scales. Two types of SLR rate are present: one is the SLR rate in the laboratory frame of reference (i.e., static), T 1 ; the other one is in the rotation frame of reference, T 1 ρ , in which the frame of reference rotates around the axis of the external magnetic /uniFB01 eld B 0 ( z -axis) with the Larmor frequency under the applied magnetic /uniFB01 eld B 1 .

T 1 is sensitive to ion motion of targeted nuclei close to the Larmor frequency ( ω 0 ), which is determined by ω 0 = γ B 0 ( γ is magnetogyric ratio). It mainly reveals the local fast motion with the jump frequency in the range of 10 7 -10 9 s -1 . T 1 ρ can be used to probe slower motion with a frequency of several tens of kHz under selected lock-in /uniFB01 elds B 1 ( ω 1 = γ B 1 ) or rotation frame of reference. 185 -188 The SSR rate ( T 2 ) is sensitive to even slower motion and the corresponding relaxation time is on the order of 10 -3 s or even slower. Therefore, motional processes at a broad range of time scales can be e /uniFB00 ectively probed using these three types of relaxation time measurements. The analysis of T 2 is very complex because T 2 is related to not only the high-frequency motion but also the low-frequency motion and various other factors during the measurement, such as magnetic /uniFB01 eld inhomogeneity. Therefore, T 1 and T 1 ρ analyses are often used in NMR relaxometry methods.

In SLR experiments, the SLR times, T 1 or T 1 ρ , are measured as a function of temperature, as shown in Figure 10b. The log(1/ T 1 ) (or log(1/ T 1 ρ )) vs 1/ T curves usually reach a maximum at a critical temperature, and the rate τ -1 of ion motion at this temperature can be obtained using ω 0 τ ≈ 1 (or ω 1 τ ≈ 0.5), where ω 0 and ω 1 are the Larmor frequency in the laboratory and rotation frame, respectively. These curves are usually symmetric for a 3D isotropic uncorrelated hopping process and exhibit asymmetry because of correlation e /uniFB00 ects or the reduced dimensionality of the ion transport process. 170,189 The correlation e /uniFB00 ects can be understood using Bloembergen -Purcell -Pound models (BPP models), 168 which account for the Coulombic interactions and structural disorder. The entire curve can be separated into a low-temperature /uniFB02 ank and a hightemperature /uniFB02 ank. In general, the low-temperature /uniFB02 ank contains information on local ion transport, whereas the hightemperature /uniFB02 ank is relevant to long-range ion di /uniFB00 usion. Analysis of the slopes of the high- and low-temperature /uniFB02 anks, activation barriers at di /uniFB00 erent length scales, and di /uniFB00 usion path topology at the relevant time scales can be obtained. Furthermore, by comparing the slopes of the low- and hightemperature /uniFB02 anks, β = E a,low/ E a,high can be obtained, revealing correlation e /uniFB00 ects of ion transport processes, e.g., in Li 6PS5Br, 190

3.1.1.3. Two-Dimensional Exchange NMR. Two-dimensional exchange NMR (2D NMR) makes it possible to observe ion jumps between di /uniFB00 erent crystallographic sites. 196,197 In a typical 2D exchange NMR measurement, the nuclei spins are /uniFB01 rst excited and allowed to ' talk ' with each other during a userde /uniFB01 ned mixing time. The interaction between di /uniFB00 erent nuclei spins is encoded in the free induction decay (FID) signal. Speci /uniFB01 cally speaking, the resonance frequency ω ( t ′ ) of a certain ion at time t ′ is correlated to the resonance frequency ω ( t ″ ) after a mixing time τ ( τ = t ″ -t ′ ). If there is no ion jump during the mixing time, ω ( t ′ ) = ω ( t ″ ), only signals along the diagonal intensities are observed. If a jump to another site occurs during the mixing time, then ω ( t ′ ) ≠ ω ( t ″ ), leading to o /uniFB00 -diagonal intensities. This o /uniFB00 -diagonal signal is directly related to the jump between two di /uniFB00 erent lattice sites, which can be observed in many structures, e.g., in Li-ion conductors Li 7 PSe6, 176 and Li2ZrO3 (Figure 11a,b). 198 According to simple /uniFB01 rst-order kinetics, the o /uniFB00 -diagonal signal intensities can be analyzed as a function of mixing time employing eq 10.

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

Here, I ij is the o /uniFB00 -diagonal signal intensity after the mixing and I 0 is the diagonal signal intensity before mixing, τ ij is the time for a jump from position i to j and τ m is the mixing time. Sometimes, although the o /uniFB00 -diagonal signal intensity cannot be observed, broadening of the diagonal intensity does appear. For garnet structure Li5La3Nb2O12, 196 upon increasing the mixing time from 200 ms to 1 s, no o /uniFB00 -diagonal intensity can be observed but broadening of the diagonal signal is noticeable (Figure 11c,d). This broadening of the diagonal signal suggests that at the time scale of 200 ms to 1 s, the jump in this material occurs between the octahedral sites not the tetrahedral sites. Because of technical limitations, the jump time τ can be probed in 2D exchange NMR is usually within 10 -4 to 1 s, similar to the time scale accessible from impedance measurements. Finally, we note that the NMR resolution is intrinsically low for ion conductors with fast ion motion and diamagnetic nature. Higher magnetic /uniFB01 eld, lower temperature, and the selection of a more appropriate probing isotope (e.g., 6 Li vs 7 Li) are all crucial for a successful 2D exchange NMR measurement.

3.1.1.4. Quasi-Elastic Neutron Scattering. Quasi-elastic neutron scattering (QENS) probes ion di /uniFB00 usion and rotation processes on the atomic scale, although a large amount of samples (typically ∼ 10 g) is often required. 199 Neutrons can interact with atomic nuclei via elastic and inelastic scattering. For pure elastic scattering, the energy transfer between source neutrons and the sample is equal to 0, whereas inelastic scattering involves relatively large energy transfer. When the interaction between neutrons and the sample occurs with small energy transfer near 0 (usually ± 2 meV), such interaction is called quasi-elastic scattering, as illustrated in Figure 12a. 199 In QENS measurements, the intensity of scattered neutrons is measured as a function of momentum transfer Q and energy transfer ω , plotted as the scattering function S ( Q , ω ). The /uniFB01 nite resolution of the instrument is measured as the peak width of the elastic scattering function. The broadening of a quasi-elastic scattering function is indicative of ion di /uniFB00 usion in materials, as illustrated in Figure 12b for the Li-ion conductor 7 Li 7 P3S11 crystal 200 at elevated temperatures. QENS is sensitive to motion processes at the time scale of 10 -8 to 10 -12 s. 201 From QENS measurements, parameters related to ion dynamics at the atomic scale, such as the ion jump distance, jump frequency, and random di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient D r , can be obtained. 201,202

Neutron scattering can be either coherent or incoherent depending on whether the scattered neutron waves from di /uniFB00 erent scattering centers interfere with each other. Coherent scattering refers to the case when interference occurs between scattered neutrons from di /uniFB00 erent atoms, from which the information on crystal structure can be resolved, such as in neutron di /uniFB00 raction; incoherent scattering means no interference between scattered neutrons, therefore, single-atom motion can be investigated. The strength of neutron coherent and incoherent scattering for elements are quanti /uniFB01 ed by their scattering lengths b (shown in Table 3 for selected elements)

For other elements that the isotope is not speci /uniFB01 ed, their scattering lengths are weightedaverage of the respective isotopes and cross sections σ s , where σ π = ⟨| |⟩ b 4 s 2 . Because the incoherent neutron scattering cross-section of hydrogen is signi /uniFB01 cantly larger than that of many other elements, hydrogen is the dominant incoherent scatterer in most hydrogen-containing compounds causing self-di /uniFB00 usion. Therefore, QENS has been widely utilized in investigations of proton conductors such as hydrated BaZrO3, 203,204 BaCeO3, 205 and La0.8 Ba1.2 GaO3.9. 206 Analysis of the di /uniFB00 usion of lithium ions using QENS requires more stringent experimental conditions than that of proton conductors because of the low incoherent cross-section of lithium. 207,208 7 Li enriched materials are often necessary for high quality QENS measurement because of the small incoherent scattering cross-section and high neutron absorptivity of 6 Li (9.4 × 10 -26 m 2 ). Recently, the coherent scattering signal of oxygen has been applied in QENS to study the oxide ion di /uniFB00 usion. 209,210

Ion di /uniFB00 usion in grains has been described by several models. 212 For instance, the classical Chudley -Elliott (CE) model 213 is based on the assumption that an ion jump occurs in a lattice in random directions with a constant jump distance a . The half-width at half-maximum (HWHM) Γ of the scattering function is given by

Figure 13. (a) The time-dependent positron count N(t) (solid line), and the average (isotropic) exponential decay (dashed line). Reproduced with permission from ref 225. Reproduced with permission from ref 226. Copyright 2009 American Physical Society.

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

̈

where Q is the momentum transfer, τ -1 is the jump frequency, and a is the average jump distance. In the Singwi -Sjo ander (SS) model, 214 an ion jump is assumed to occur alternatively between oscillatory motion and directed motion with an exponential distribution of jump distance. Finally, in the Hall -Ross (HR) model, 215 the ion jump is assumed to occur in a restricted volume with a Gaussian distribution of jump distance.

By /uniFB01 tting the experimental data using the di /uniFB00 usion models, the jump distance and jump frequency can be determined. 200,217 From the derived jump distances, the di /uniFB00 usion pathway can be veri /uniFB01 ed by referencing to the crystallographic distances between mobile ion sites. 205,218,219 In the perovskite-type Yb-doped SrCeO3 proton conductor, the jump distance of protons was determined to be 4.0 Å, close to the mean O -Odistance (3.2 Å) of the reported crystal structure. 218 Similarly, for lithium conductors Li7P3S11, Mori et al. 200 reported that the lithium jump distance coincide with the average bond length between Li ions.

With jump frequency and jump distance in hand, the random di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient D r can be obtained using Einstein -Smoluchowski relation (eq 9). 199 For small Q , HWHM is approximated as ΔΓ ( Q ) = D r Q 2 . For large Q , by /uniFB01 tting the Q 2 -dependent HWHM according to the SS model, the di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cients D r of Li-ion conductors 7 Li7P3S11 200 and Li5La3Ta2O12 208 at around 473 K were calculated to be 5.7 × 10 -10 m 2 s -1 (Figure 12c) and 10 -10 m 2 s -1 , respectively. Analogous to other techniques, the activation barrier E a of ion motion in fast-ionic conductor was derived by using an Arrhenius plot. 205 -220

In addition, the local ion motion within a few Angstroms can be investigated by QENS via the elastic incoherent scattering factor (EISF), extracted from the ratio of elastic intensity to the sumofelastic and inelastic intensity. The elastic line is originated from neutron di /uniFB00 raction with the nuclei of the scattering species over a /uniFB01 nite volume by its motion. 216 Therefore, the EISF can be used to di /uniFB00 erentiate between local ion motions, as displayed in Figure 12d, e.g., a rotational jump between two sites (I), rotational di /uniFB00 usion on the surface of a sphere of radius R (II), rotational di /uniFB00 usion on the circumference of a circle of radius R (III), and di /uniFB00 usion within the volume of a sphere of radius R (IV). The EISF data of t h e proton conductor SrCe0.95 Yb0.05 H0.02 O2.985 revealed that during the proton residence time at an oxygen site, the proton rotates around its attached oxygen for approximately 50 revolutions before jumping and attaching to the next oxygen ion. 221 The EISF of polycrystalline BaZr0.85 Y0.15 O2.975 · H2O is similar to curve I in Figure 12d, which indicated a rotational jump between two sites, yielding a jump distance of a = 3.1 Å. 219

Temperature-dependent ion reorientation motion can be investigated using neutron /uniFB01 xed-window scans (NFWSs). 222 In the NFWS, only the intensity of elastic-scattered neutrons with zero-energy transfer is measured as a function of temperature. At low temperatures, the rate of ion reorientation motion is low; hence, the quasi-elastic component is too narrow to be separated from the pure elastic component with /uniFB01 nite resolution. In this case, it is di /uniFB03 cult to distinguish elastic and quasi-elastic components; therefore, the intensity of elastic scattered neutrons is maximized (normalized to 1 in Figure 12e). However, at high temperatures, the rate of reorientation motion is high, resulting in a wider quasi-elastic component than the energy resolution of the instrument. Under these circumstances, the quasi-elastic component collapses and contributes no signi /uniFB01 cant intensity to that of elastic components at zero energy transfer in FWS. At moderate temperatures, the increasing rate of reorientation leads to a noticeable decrease in quasi-elastic scattering intensity at zero energy transfer. NFWS curves usually consist of a step-like decrease of the elastic intensity as a function of the temperature. 223 In the temperature-dependent NFWS for the Na-ion conductor Na2B12H12 displayed in Figure 12e, the onset temperature of approximately 240 -250 K after ballmilling indicates that the B12H12 2 -anion reorientation frequency reaches the order of 10 8 s -1 according to the neutron energy transfer. 224

3.1.1.5. Muon Spin Relaxation. Muon spin relaxation ( μ + SR) is a characterization technique that analyzes the e /uniFB00 ects of implanted spin-polarized muons on a material. The sensitivity of implanted positive muons ( μ + ) to the local magnetism and the time evolution of spin polarization makes this technique capable of probing the ion di /uniFB00 usion in solids. The ion di /uniFB00 usion in the sample perturbs the embedded muon, allowing the di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient to be extracted from analysis of the perturbation. Comparing with QENS, the sample mass required for μ + SR measurement is much smaller. 225 μ + SR has been successfully applied to study lithium and sodium ion transport, 226 -237 which require long data collection time by QENS, although most of these studies are related to battery electrode materials (i.e., mixed conductors) and only a few cases were found for pure ionic conductors. In the following, we will focus on the

extraction of the random di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient and activation energy from μ + SR, with examples from both solid-state electrolyte and electrode materials.

Muonspin relaxation is caused by the asymmetry of the spatial distribution of positrons produced by the decay of polarized particles. Ion di /uniFB00 usion a /uniFB00 ects the local nuclear magnetic /uniFB01 eld and the muon decay behavior for positron production. By measuring the asymmetry and time-domain spectrum of positrons generated by the decay, we can obtain the polarization function in the sample, so as to explore ion transport properties of the sample, shown in Figure 13a. μ + SR can be used to examine the ionic dynamics in the intrinsic crystalline lattice without extrinsic interferences, such as those from the grain boundaries or defects, as occurs in the traditional electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) technique. 229 It is favorable to locally resolve the intragrain ions di /uniFB00 usion at the atomic scale, with little restriction on the sample size. The μ + SR technique can measure magnetic /uniFB02 uctuation rates in the range of 10 4 -10 12 Hz, depending on the magnitude of the magnetic /uniFB01 eld at the muon site. This characteristic time window bridges the gap between /uniFB02 uctuation rates detected by NMR and QENS techniques (also shown in Figure 2).

In the garnet Li 5+ x La3Zr x Nb2 -x O12, lithium ionic conductivity was observed to vary by 7.5 times as a function of Zr content x , while D r remained almost independent of x . This /uniFB01 nding suggests that ionic conductivity is heavily dependent on the mobile lithium ion concentration, which varies substantially through composition tuning in Li 5+ x La3Zr x Nb2 -x O12. 232

The activation energies probed by μ + SR can be much smaller than that of EIS. 218 -237,239 The di /uniFB00 erent results obtained from these two techniques can be originated from the fact that μ + SR probes intragrain ion transport, whereas EIS measures the ion transport both in grain and across grain boundaries, which can be in /uniFB02 uenced by the sample densi /uniFB01 cation process.

3.1.2. Macroscopic Ion Transport. Macroscopic ion transport processes occur at relatively large time scale (10 -10 to 10 s) and length scales. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) is the most common method to probe macroscopic ion transport processes. EIS measures conductivity under electric /uniFB01 eld perturbation, through which the ion di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient D σ can be determined if the mobile ion concentration is known. With careful measurements and data analysis, EIS can be used to distinguish contribution between ions and electrons 240 -242 and to tell the ionic transport process within the grain and across grain boundaries. While EIS is not element speci /uniFB01 c, tracer di /uniFB00 usion and pulsed /uniFB01 eld gradient NMR are element speci /uniFB01 c and can be used to directly measure tracer di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cients D * . Using the ionic conductivity determined by EIS and D * by tracer di /uniFB00 usion or PFG NMR, the mobile ion concentration can be in principle obtained. Below, we elaborate tracer di /uniFB00 usion and PFG NMR measurements and some recent advances in impedance measurement, with examples of oxide-, lithium-, and silver-ion conductors. In the discussion section 5.4, we tabulate the mobile ion concentrations for several systems for which both ionic conductivity and di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient have been measured.

3.1.2.1. Tracer Di /uniFB00 usion. In tracer di /uniFB00 usion, the isotope (e.g., 2 Hand 18 O) is di /uniFB00 used into the sample to replace the target ions at a user-de /uniFB01 ned temperature and time period, followed by isotope detection. Isotope detection is most commonly performed using time-of/uniFB02 ight secondary ion mass spectroscopy (TOF-SIMS), from which the tracer di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient D * can be directly obtained. However, other spatially resolved isotopesensitive techniques, such as other mass spectrometry techniques, 243 magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) or neutron depth pro /uniFB01 ling (NDP), can also be used. The correlation
