By plotting C vs x , the tracer di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient D * can be obtained.

Below, we present examples of isotope detection using SIMS; more details are available in Becker ' s work. 243 SIMS is a surface analysis technique that can be used to characterize the quantity of isotopes on a material surface. It is based on resolving the atomic mass to charge ( m / z ) ratio of ejected particles under ion bombardment. A schematic diagram of the SIMS measurement is shown in Figure 14a. After sputtering away a constant thickness of material from sample surface, the secondary ions are knocked out by the primary ion beam (blue particles in Figure 14a). The intensity of ejected ion species is recorded as a function of the m / z ratio. By repeating this process, the depth pro /uniFB01 le of di /uniFB00 erent element isotopes can be tracked.

Therefore, the tracer di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient and activation energy can be obtained by SIMS. In Figure 14b, from the oxygen d e p t h p r o /uniFB01 l e o f t h e o x i d e - i o n c o n d u c t o r (Y2O3)2(Sc2O3)9(ZrO2)89 with and without a silver layer (silver was added to increase the rate of surface oxygen exchange), the tracer di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient D * was observed to fall in the range from 10 -8 to 10 -7 cm 2 s -1 between 600 and 900 ° C. The activation energy E a was determined to be 0.9 eV through a linear /uniFB01 tting of ln( D * ) vs 1/ T . 245

One of the major di /uniFB03 culties in measuring the tracer di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient is that the isotope is usually introduced into the sample in the vapor or liquid phase, which currently limits the technique to proton and oxide-ion conductors. In such measurements, water and other H or O residual groups must be removed for the accuracy of the experimental results. 246 If vapor/liquid phases containing Li, Na, Mg, F, etc., can be applied, tracer di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cients can be in principle measured for a wide variety of ionic conductors. In addition to solid -gas and solid -liquid isotope exchange, solid -solid isotope exchange is also possible. Hu et al. 247 assembled a symmetric battery cell based on two 6 Li foil electrodes. By applying a constant current, 6 Li will be stripped from one end and deposited onto the other 6 Li metal electrode depending on the direction of the current. Thus, a concentration gradient of 6 Li at the interface between two 6 Li foils and the solid electrolyte can be created, enabling further analysis using spatially resolved techniques such as MRI and SIMS. We believe that a similar method can be employed to study Na and Mg conductors, for which a symmetric battery cell can be built upon these alkali and alkaline-earth metals.

3.1.2.2. Pulsed Field Gradient Nuclear Magnetic Resonance. The concept of a gradient /uniFB01 eld was proposed in the 1950s, when it was observed that the use of spin echo technology could eliminate the nonuniform broadening of spectral lines caused by a gradient magnetic /uniFB01 eld as the broad component does not give any echo signal. The most common pulse sequence for the PFG NMR method is shown in Figure 15a. In PFG NMR experiment, two gradient pulses (a gradient magnetization is applied along the z -direction) with duration δ and interval Δ are added to the simple echo pulse (i.e., a 90 ° radio frequency (RF) pulse followed by a 180 ° RF pulse). By virtue of these two gradient pulses, long-range di /uniFB00 usion processes can be probed. The NMR echo amplitudes for simple echo measurement and for that with gradient pulses are di /uniFB00 erent only if long-range di /uniFB00 usion of the probed ion (e.g., Li) occurs at

Copyright 2013 American Chemical Society. (c) Measurement principle to detect the bulk and grain boundary conductivity. Reproduced with permission from ref 256. Copyright 2003 Elsevier BV. (d) Impedance spectra of an undoped AgCl polycrystal measured by microelectrodes on a grain and grain boundary at room temperature, respectively. Reproduced with permission from ref 256. Copyright 2003 Elsevier BV.

the relevant PFG NMR time scale. The tracer di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient D * can be calculated via Stejskal and Tanner equation: 248,249

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

where E and E 0 are the signal intensity at t = 0 and t = 2 τ , respectively, γ is the nuclear magnetogyric ratio, g is the PFG strength, δ is the duration of the gradient /uniFB01 eld, and Δ is the di /uniFB00 usion time (or the interval between two gradient pulses). By plotting the logarithm E / E 0 vs -γ 2 g 2 δ 2 ( Δ -δ /3), the di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient can be obtained from the slope of the linear /uniFB01 tting.

PFG NMR techniques are generally sensitive to the translational movement of probed ions at time scales of 10 -2 to 1 s and a length scale of approximately 10 μ m; hence, this technique can be used to probe long-range transport. The Li-ion di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient D * obtained from 7 Li PFG NMR echo attenuation curves of LGPS 36,178 is consistent with the extrapolated experimental di /uniFB00 usivity from ionic conductivity measurements and the computed di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient Dab assuming that Li + di /uniFB00 usion only occurs in the ab plane, as shown in Figure 15b. This /uniFB01 nding demonstrates that macroscopic Li transport in LGPS is predominantly a result of microscopic di /uniFB00 usion along the ab plane.

By tuning g and Δ , di /uniFB00 usion processes at di /uniFB00 erent time and length scales can be probed. Previous works have clearly demonstrated that a wide distribution of di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cients exists at di /uniFB00 erent time and length scales even in the same material. In (Li 2 S)7 (P2S5)3 , Hayamizu et al. characterized lithium ionic di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cients D * using PFG NMR. As shown in Figure 15c, at a /uniFB01 xed gradient strength g = 9.4 Tm -1 , echo attenuation (i.e., E / E 0) was measured as a function of di /uniFB00 usion time, changing from 25 to 75 ms. At short di /uniFB00 usion time (purple line), a perfect linear correlation between E / E 0 and -γ 2 g 2 δ 2 ( Δ -δ /3) was observed. As the di /uniFB00 usion time increased, the deviation from perfect linearity became increasingly pronounced. When the di /uniFB00 usion time (i.e., Δ ) became longer, the average speed of ion transport was reduced (i.e., a smaller slope as observed in Figure 15c). This /uniFB01 nding suggests that the di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cients di /uniFB00 er at di /uniFB00 erent length scales. Similarly, if the di /uniFB00 usion time is set at 30 ms, upon increasing the gradient strength from 7.3 to 17.4 Tm -1 , to follow di /uniFB00 usion processes at decreasing time scale, deviation from linearity was also observed. Below 14.9 Tm -1 , an exponential function remained su /uniFB03 cient to /uniFB01 t the PFG NMR curve, which suggests that lithium-ion di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cients are polydisperse and may vary smoothly as a function of length scales. Measurement with smaller g probes transport of the lithium ions at a faster rate, and thus over a longer transport distance during the same di /uniFB00 usion time Δ . We further note that the ion transport distance is assumed to be the mean free path, which is de /uniFB01 ned in gas kinetics. However, for the largest g at 17.4 Tm -1 , the echo attenuation curve cannot be /uniFB01 t even by an exponential function. Rather, strong /uniFB02 uctuation was observed in the attenuation curve, especially in the region marked with blue dashed circles in Figure 15d. This /uniFB02 uctuation likely indicates the presence of an additional ion transport process at a similar time scale with dramatically di /uniFB00 erent di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cients, which may correspond to ion transport across grain boundaries or collisions with the immobile anion groups.

3.1.2.3. Electrochemical Impedance Spectroscopy. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) is one of the most important and widely used techniques to measure the ionic conductivity of materials. The activation energy can be evaluated by variable-temperature EIS, and the long-range di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient D σ can be calculated by the Einstein -Nernst eq (eq 4) if the mobile ion concentration is also known. In section

5.3, we show detailed examples to emphasize that log( σ T ) vs 1/ T should be plotted instead of log( σ ) to estimate activation energy and prefactor.

Acommonwaytomeasureimpedance spectra is by applying a sinusoidal voltage signal as a function of frequency on sample and measuring the current response. 250 A typical impedance instrument can measure impedance in the range of 10 -2 and 10 8 Ω . If a high resistance analyzer is equipped, a broader range can be accessed. The electrochemical process including ionic motion can be resolved by measuring the frequency-dependent electrical conductivity and /uniFB01 tting the impedance spectra with an electric circuit model.

Impedance spectra are usually complicated by the presence of both grains and grain boundaries which exhibit di /uniFB00 erent ion transport properties. Because most ionic conductors are polycrystalline, it is important to understand the role of grain boundaries on the ionic motion. The brick layer model is the most commonly accepted model for quantitative separation of grain boundary contribution from bulk contribution. 251 In this model, all grain boundaries are assumed to be identical. 252 On the basis of the brick layer model, the grain and grain boundary contributions to Li-ion conductivity were distinguished from the complex impedance plane in Li10SnP2S12 when lowering the temperature to -120 ° C, whereas at above -60 ° C, the detection of bulk response was out of the limit of the impedance analyzer, 253 as shown in Figure 16a,b. More accurate analysis of bulk resistance at a higher temperature range can be obtained from the modulus plot, as demonstrated for Li-ion conductor Li 1+ x Al x Ti2 -x (PO4)3. 254

The microelectrode-based method, on the other hand, can measure intragrain ion transport irrespective of microstructural e /uniFB00 ects, such as grain boundaries and sample density. 255 Because the microstructural e /uniFB00 ects were excluded, a 3 orders of magnitude increase in the Li + bulk conductivity (from 3.16 × 10 -6 to 5.63 × 10 -3 S cm -1 ) was reported by doping 0.1 -0.15 mol Al in LiTi2(PO4)3 (LTP) single crystals, 252,255 which was attributed to additional Li + sites at the M3 (Wycko /uniFB00 36 f ) introduced by dopants. Therefore, a fast-conducting pathway is opened by the displacement of Li + at M1 sites (Wycko /uniFB00 6 b ) to the nearest-neighboring M3 sites. In the work of AgCl polycrystal, by positioning the probe on top of a grain or grain boundary area, the grain and grain boundary silver ionic conductivities are distinguished, as shown in Figure 16c,d. 256 Microelectrodes were further applied to investigate the anisotropic distribution of phases in nitrogen-doped zirconia according to the local nitrogen conductivity. 257

Despite of the widespread utilization of EIS, a recently interlaboratory study 258 has pointed out the relative uncertainty when reproducing impedance measurements in thiophosphatebased electrolytes. This work emphasized the need to more rigorously consider the measurement conditions when comparing the impedance data between di /uniFB00 erent works, such as a uniform sample geometry, density, measurement atmosphere, etc.

## 3.2. Structure

3.2.1. Long-Range Structure. Only ions migrating over a rather long distance can result in observable ionic conductivity, which is primarily determined by the long-range structure of the material. X-ray, neutron, and electron di /uniFB00 raction can all be used to probe long-range structures. From Bragg ' s law, the lattice volume and lattice parameters can be obtained. With highquality di /uniFB00 raction data, e.g., single-crystal and synchrotron data, subtler information that a /uniFB00 ect ion transport processes is accessible, such as the polyhedral volume, bottleneck size, anisotropic thermal motion of atoms (indicative of migration pathway), and atom coordinates. Below, we discuss the characterizations of long-range structures of lithium, oxygen, sodium, copper, and silver ion conductors and the correlations between long-range structures and ion transport properties.

3.2.1.1. X-ray Di /uniFB00 raction. X-ray di /uniFB00 raction (XRD) is one of the most commonly adopted tools for characterizing the longrange, averaged structure of materials and can be performed on both powder and single-crystal samples. Single-crystal di /uniFB00 raction provides frames (or 2D slices) of the reciprocal space, through which part of the reciprocal space (determined by instrumental resolution) can be reconstructed in three dimensions (3D). In powder di /uniFB00 raction, however, 3D information on the reciprocal space is collapsed into a simple curve of Bragg scattering intensity vs 2 θ (or d spacing or scattering vector Q ). Analysis of such a powder di /uniFB00 raction pattern is intrinsically much more challenging than that of single crystal di /uniFB00 raction, especially for fast ionic conductors that are rich in disorder and distortions.

With a commonly used Cu K α ( λ = 1.5406 Å) X-ray source and XRD scans with 2 θ up to 90 ° , the smallest d spacing that can be probed is approximately 1.1 Å (if at the upper 2 θ limit, the signal remains three times stronger than background /uniFB02 uctuation, i.e., I &gt; 3 σ ). With such quality of data, usually the lattice symmetry and lattice parameters can be reliably extracted, but other structural parameters, such as atomic coordinates or thermal parameters, cannot. In contrast, synchrotron XRD usually exhibits excellent counting statistics even at a d -spacing much lower than 1 Å (e.g., 0.6 Å), enabling accurate determination of site occupancies and anisotropic thermal parameters for heavy scatters. However, for light elements (such as H, Li, and even O), such detailed structural parameters are still unattainable. Polyhedral volumes, site disorders, and other structural factors have been investigated in a series of studies with the help of X-ray powder di /uniFB00 raction (XRPD) re /uniFB01 nement and related to ion transport properties, providing structural insights for the design of high-conductivity ionic conductors. 103 Additionally, peak splitting is indicative of symmetry descending toward lower symmetry. 259 -261 Changes in the relative peak intensities may arise from site disorder or polyhedral size variations. 8 In some works, stacking and coloring faults of ionic conductors with layered honeycomb structure have been studied by re /uniFB01 nement of XRPD data. 262 -264 Stacking and coloring faults are common in layered materials. Stacking faults, which arise from irregular stacking of layers, often result in asymmetry of di /uniFB00 raction peaks. Coloring faults represent stacking disorder without variations in the local octahedral orientations or site symmetry. By contrast, these variations are always coupled with stacking faults. In the work on Na-ion conductors by Seibel et al., the coloring e /uniFB00 ect originating from B ′ (Sn i n Na3 -x Sn2 -x Sb x NaO6) and B ′′ (Na i n Na3 -x Sn2 -x Sb x NaO6) sites mixing was derived to be approximately 21% of the total faulting by re /uniFB01 ning the site occupancies. More complicated defects, such as the number faults proposed by Khalifah and co-workers, can also be present in honeycomb layered materials. 265,266

̅

Single-crystal X-ray di /uniFB00 raction (SC-XRD) remains the best choice for structural determination of fast ionic conductors. 267 -269 Subtle monoclinic distortion can be resolved in Li3La3W2O12 by SC-XRD 100 from splitting of the 101 and 101 peaks, which is otherwise undetectable in laboratory XRPD data.

Figure 17. (a) Crystal structure obtained from re /uniFB01 nement results of single crystal X-ray di /uniFB00 raction data and proposed di /uniFB00 usion pathways based on anisotropic thermal parameters in Na11Sn2PS12. Reproduced with permission from ref 267. Copyright 2018 American Chemical Society. (b) Anisotropic thermal parameters from re /uniFB01 nement results of single crystal X-ray di /uniFB00 raction data, displayed as ellipsoids, indicative of di /uniFB00 usion pathway. Reproduced with permission from ref 267. Copyright 2018 American Chemical Society.

Using SC-XRD, Ramos et al. 267 observed a redistribution of Na + in Na11Sn2SbS12 (NSSS) compared with isostructural Na11Sn2PS12 (NSPS). For the same Na content, Na + ions are depopulated from framework sites present in NSPS to occupy an additional interstitial Na6 site in NSSS, which possesses high site energy and function as an interconnection site for Na migration, as illustrated by the arrows in Figure 17a. The depopulation of Na from the framework sites leads to higher fraction of vacancy in major di /uniFB00 usion pathways. The resulting preferred sodium di /uniFB00 usion pathways of Na 11Sn2SbS12 marked in Figure 17a were proposed on the basis of the re /uniFB01 ned anisotropic thermal parameters of Na + , as displayed of ellipsoids in Figure 17b. A lower ionic conductivity, nevertheless, resulted from the stronger electrostatic interaction between Na + and S 2 -due to the more ionic Sb -S bonds in Na 11Sn2SbS12 than in NSPS. The ionic nature of the bonds was speculated based on the electronegativity di /uniFB00 erences between Sb (1.82, Allred -Rochow scale), P (2.06, Allred -Rochow scale), and S (2.44, Allred -Rochow scale). This work provides insights into the complex e /uniFB00 ect of substitution on the properties of ion transport. In some cases, even the positions of Li can be determined from singlecrystal XRD data. Awaka et al. located the Li positions in t -Li7La3Zr2O12 using a Fourier di /uniFB00 erence map. 270 In addition, a modulated structure resulting from lithium chain ordering was determined for the battery cathode LiFeBO 3 using single-crystal XRD. 271

To summarize, with synchrotron X-ray sources, powder di /uniFB00 raction can be used to accurately determine structural parameters such as atomic coordinates, site occupancies, and thermal parameters (except for light elements such as H and Li). 272 -274 From this information, the bond length can then be derived from accurate atomic coordinates, and the ion jump distances and extents of cation -cation and cation -anion electrostatic interactions can be estimated. 31 Similarly, the polyhedral volume, bottleneck size, and coordination environment can also be determined. 275,276 From single-crystal di /uniFB00 raction, detailed structural parameters for light elements, such as Li, can also be derived, which is crucial for understanding the structure and ion transport correlations for a wide range of materials, including Li + , O 2 -, and F -conductors.

3.2.1.2. Neutron Di /uniFB00 raction. Neutron di /uniFB00 raction is in many senses complementary to X-ray di /uniFB00 raction. Neutron di /uniFB00 raction is based on physics similar to that of X-ray di /uniFB00 raction, except for a di /uniFB00 erent set of atomic scattering powers. 211,277 Unlike X-ray di /uniFB00 raction, scattering lengths and hence scattering cross sections (scale with the power of scattering length) of neutron di /uniFB00 raction are not proportional to electron density (or atomic number). Therefore, neutron di /uniFB00 raction can probe light elements such as H, Li, and Na and distinguish between isotopes and elements with similar X-ray cross sections (such as Fe/Mn and Sn/Sb). The scattering factors of di /uniFB00 erent atoms with neutrons also decays much slower than with X-rays with respect to the d -spacing; therefore, information at a d -spacing below 0.5 Å is usually attainable using neutron di /uniFB00 raction.

Neutron powder di /uniFB00 raction can be used to locate light atoms such as Li (Figure 18a). 50,269,278 -280 In Li 1.3 Al0.3 Ti1.7(PO4)3 (LATP), a structural model without any lithium, was /uniFB01 rst re /uniFB01 ned against the neutron powder di /uniFB00 raction data, based on which Fourier di /uniFB00 erence map was calculated. 278 Because of the negative coherent scattering length of Li ( -1.90 f m ) 211 with neutrons, the negative intensities in the Fourier di /uniFB00 erence map are indicative of the exact positions of Li within the LATP structure. As inferred in Figure 18a, very strong negative intensities were observed at (0,0,0) and (0.11, 0.33, 0.08) (marked as Li1 and Li3), corresponding to Li occupancy at these two sites. This redistribution of Li + to Li3 sites has also been reported elsewhere 50 and has been attributed to additional electrostatic repulsion induced with increasing Li content. 281,282 However, we note that Ti is also a negative scatterer in neutron di /uniFB00 raction. Whether positions with negative intensities indeed correspond to Li should be analyzed further. Bond valence analysis is potentially useful in this regard. Not only the position of lithium or sodium atoms but also the lightest element H can be identi /uniFB01 ed in deuterated samples (the coherent scattering length is 6.67 f m for 2 H) 211 from neutron di /uniFB00 raction, e.g., in proton conductors with antiperovskite structure, leading to the identi /uniFB01 cation of hydroxide ( -OH) bond orientations. 283,284 Eilbracht et al. 285 discovered hydroxide ( -OH) bond orientations in antiperovskite lithium ion conductors Li 2 Br(OD) and its high-temperature modi /uniFB01 cation LiBr · D2O, using a Fourier di /uniFB00 erence map for a certain plane (the triangular OLi 6 octahedral surface), as displayed in Figure 18b. In Li2Br(OD), deuterium atoms were observed to be located at 12-fold sites, thus having OH bonds pointing toward edges of OLi 6 octahedra, whereas OH bonds in LiBr · D2O pointed toward octahedral faces. Combined with 2 H-NMR results obtained in the same

Figure 18. (a) Exact positions of Li within LATP structure derived from Fourier di /uniFB00 erence map analysis of neutron powder di /uniFB00 raction data. Because of negative scattering of Li under neutron di /uniFB00 raction, locations with negative intensity indicate the presence of Li atoms (Li1 (0,0,0), Li3 (0.11, 0.33, 0.08)). Reproduced with permission from ref 278. Copyright 2016 American Chemical Society. (b) In Fourier di /uniFB00 erence maps derived from neutron di /uniFB00 raction in a certain plane (triangular OLi6 octahedral surface), enabling identi /uniFB01 cation of orientations of the -OD group in antiperovskite Li 2 Br(OD) and its high-temperature modi /uniFB01 cation LiBr · D2O. Reproduced with permission from ref 285. Copyright 1997 Published by Elsevier BV. (c) Anisotropic lithium vibration in c -Li7La3Zr2O12 derived from re /uniFB01 nement of neutron di /uniFB00 raction data, indicating preferred di /uniFB00 usion pathways along Li(24 d ) -Li(96 h ) -Li(48 g ) -Li(96 h ) -Li(24 d ). Reproduced with permission from ref 288. Copyright 2012 Royal Society of Chemistry.

work, 12-fold hopping of deuterons between OLi6 octahedral edges was deduced. In Y-doped BaZrO3 proton conductor, neutron powder di /uniFB00 raction revealed evidence of deuterium superstructure, which is linked to the superstructure of yttrium dopant discovered by Y-resonant XRD. 286 The ordered dopant structures were predicted to strongly enhance the proton conductivity. 287

Isotropic/anisotropic thermal parameters of atoms are often indicative of preferred directions of atom motions. Using neutron di /uniFB00 raction, which is more sensitive to light elements (most mobile ions are light elements), preferred di /uniFB00 usion pathways can be inferred from thermal parameters. 279 Thermal parameters are related to the decay in intensity vs the d -spacing. Neutron di /uniFB00 raction can probe much smaller d -spacings than Xray di /uniFB00 ractions and can thus more accurately determine thermal parameters. In the garnet-type lithium-ion conductor Li7La3Zr2O12 (LLZO), 288 anisotropic thermal parameter ellipsoids pointing along the Li(24 d ) -Li(96 h ) -Li(48 g ) -Li(96 h ) -Li(24 d ) direction, as displayed in the left of Figure 18c, indicate that the preferred di /uniFB00 usion pathways of LLZO involve 24 d sites. 289 -291 Alternatively, the maximum entropy method (MEM) can also be applied to analyze the di /uniFB00 usion path, resulting in a similar conclusion (Figure 18c right). The use of MEM can result in better resolution and a non-Gaussian probability distribution of electron/nuclear density maps in contrast to the routinely used Fourier synthesis, which is critical for studies of ion dynamics (or di /uniFB00 usion). However, this method requires great caution in interpreting the data as spurious features may arise, and the MEM maps, as often displayed in user-de /uniFB01 ned iso-surfaces, can sometimes be misleading. We note that the Li-ion di /uniFB00 usion path in LATP obtained using neutron di /uniFB00 raction and MEM analyses is in fact di /uniFB00 erent from that of other works. 87,187,292 In these works, LiO4 tetrahedra were shown to be involved in the lithium migration process, similar to

Figure 19. Local structure alterations of La (1 -x )/3 Li x NbO3 upon quenching revealed by electron di /uniFB00 raction. The /uniFB01 gures at the top and bottom correspond to the pristine sample and sample after quenching, respectively. The symbol ε ′ corresponds to NbO6 octahedra tilting and nanodomain formation. Compared with the pristine sample, such tilting was observed to be suppressed after quenching. The symbols ζ and ω correspond to nanodomain disappearance and modulation of La atoms in A1 layers, respectively. Reproduced with permission from ref 304. Copyright 2018 Acta Materialia Inc.

the /uniFB01 ndings of MEM and neutron di /uniFB00 raction analysis. However, the di /uniFB00 usion path detoured from central 24 d sites and proceeded through tetrahedral corners, hopping from one octahedral site to another, according to the Li trajectories derived by MD simulations.

Upon re /uniFB01 nement, single-crystal neutron di /uniFB00 raction can provide more accurate information on site occupancies and thermal parameters than neutron powder di /uniFB00 raction and has been adopted to investigate fast ionic conductors. 293,294 Because of the ambiguity of the oxygen distribution in cubic stabilized zirconia, single-crystal time-of/uniFB02 ight neutron di /uniFB00 raction was used to investigate the composition e /uniFB00 ect on oxygen occupancies in a cubic yttria-stabilized zirconia oxygen conductor. 294,295 From the calculated Fourier di /uniFB00 erence map, the displacement of oxygen atoms to 48 g sites (in the ⟨ 100 ⟩ direction) and to a smaller extent to 32 f sites (in the ⟨ 111 ⟩ direction) was proved. Using a Fourier di /uniFB00 erence map, subtle site splitting in the oxide-ion conductor La 1 -x Ba1+ x GaO4 -x /2 can also be visualized, in which the oxide-ion conduction proceeds via the cog wheel mechanism. 133 Because of the simultaneous existence of GaO 4 and Ga2O7 groups during dynamic exchange, the O3 site with lower density corresponds to the exchanged oxygen atom during breaking and reforming of Ga2O7 groups.

3.2.1.3. Electron Di /uniFB00 raction. The scattering factors of elements in electron di /uniFB00 raction are similar to those in XRD, i.e., directly related to the electron density. However, in transmission electron microscopy (TEM), selected area electron di /uniFB00 raction (SAED) under annular dark /uniFB01 eld (ADF) mode, such as the low-angle ADF (LAADF), high-angle annular dark /uniFB01 eld (HAADF), and annular bright /uniFB01 eld (ABF) modes, is in principle locally sensitive to light elements such as H, O, and Li. 296 -300

In terms of structural information, SAED patterns can reveal rich structural details (such as structural modulation and local ordering patterns of ions and/or vacancies) similar to single- crystal di /uniFB00 racton. 301 -309 For example, in the Li-ion conductor La(1 -x )/3 Li x NbO3 perovskite, out-of-plane tilting of NbO6 octahedra was observed in the sample without quenching from SAED patterns taken in di /uniFB00 erent crystallographic directions. 301,304 The symbols of ε ′ , ζ , ω in Figure 19 indicate the presence of NbO6 octahedral tilting, twin boundaries, and modulation of La atoms, respectively. After quenching, both tilting of NbO 6 octahedra and La modulation were observed to be suppressed. These structural features are important for understanding ion transport in crystalline solids. For instance, in La(1 -x )/3 Li x NbO3, the absence of domain boundaries and strong La modulation were observed at the same time for the composition with x = 0.07, which possesses the highest ionic conductivity among all compositions. It was then speculated that La modulation might facilitate migration of Li ions. 301 However, electron di /uniFB00 raction is intrinsically statistically insigni /uniFB01 cant; therefore, structural veri /uniFB01 cations from other experimental and computational techniques are needed.

3.2.2. Short-Range Structure. Local structure can greatly a /uniFB00 ect ion transport. Pair distribution function analysis, X-ray absorption spectroscopy, and nuclear magnetic resonance spectroscopy can be used to characterize the local structure of materials. Local distortion, site disorder, coordination environment, and defects can all be studied using these tools. Below, we illustrate the use of these techniques in investigation of ion transport with examples of proton, oxygen, lithium, sodium, copper, and silver ion conductors.

3.2.2.1. Pair Distribution Function. Pair distribution function (PDF) data consists of both Bragg and di /uniFB00 use scattering information. Therefore, PDF data probes not only long-range structures, such as lattice parameters, site occupancy, and displacement parameters, but also short-range structures, such as local distortion. 310 -312 The PDF measures the probability of /uniFB01 nding pairs of atoms at a distance r , where r is

Figure 20. (a) Pair distribution function of γ -CuI (300 and 573 K) and α -CuI (673 and 773 K). In the γ -CuI system, a drastic decrease of the jump distance of Cu + ions at temperatures above the phase transition temperature was observed, with speci /uniFB01 c atom pairs assigned to peaks. Reproduced with permission from ref 311. Copyright 2008 Elsevier BV. (b) Site splitting of O4 site in La2NiO4+ δ induced by excess oxygen at interstitial positions, revealed by simulation of PDF analysis. Reproduced with permission from ref 310. Copyright 2015 Michela Brunelli and Monica Ceretti.

the radial distance in real space and is thus often indicative of jump distances for mobile ions. Take the Cu-ion conductor CuI as an example, as shown in Figure 20a, Cu + jump distances, namely the shortest Cu -Cu pair distance, were observed during the phase transition from γ -CuI to α -CuI at temperatures between 300 and 773 K (Figure 20a). 311 Pair distribution functions of CuI samples were measured. At room temperature, the /uniFB01 rst peak at approximately 2.6 Å can be decomposed into two Gaussian components at 2.61 and 2.81 Å, corresponding to Cu -I pairs with some disordered Cu sites. Upon phase transition to α -CuI at 773 K, the aforementioned peak splits into four Gaussian peaks at 2.48, 2.78, 2.95, and 2.49 Å. These arising peaks correspond to four atom pairs between the newly created 8 c (tetrahedral center) and 32 f (tetrahedral corner) sites: Cu -Cu pairs (32 f -32 f , 8 c -32 f , and 8 c -8 c ) and a Cu(32 f ) -I pair, revealing signi /uniFB01 cantly di /uniFB00 erent jump distances of Cu + ions in α -CuI.

Although PDF data /uniFB01 tting is often utilized to verify a structure against a proposed structural model using the small box method (least-squares re /uniFB01 nement, similar to Rietveld re /uniFB01 nement), 18,247 some cases indicate that describing local distortion using the small box method and an averaged structural model is inadequate. 251,313 This inadequacy stems from the highly restrained /uniFB01 tting of a small number of parameters in small box modeling. Compared with small box methods, the reverse Monte Carlo (RMC) method as a large box method allows simultaneous /uniFB01 tting in real and reciprocal space. Large models including thousands of atoms are involved in large box methods. It is thus possible to investigate various types of disorder in large box modeling. With a model crystal of suitable size, e.g., a 20 × 20 × 20 supercell for a grid size of Δ h = 0.05 rlu , the defect structure and/or disorder of materials can be determined using RMC for a number of materials. 311,314 -317 For instance, in oxide-ion conductor δ -Bi 2 O3, Hull et al. revealed a distorted anion arrangement around Bi 3+ with RMC modeling. 314 In addition, Norberg et al. utilized RMC to derive information on oxygen vacancy ordering in the oxide-ion conductor Zr0.8 Sc0.2 -x Y x O1.9 . 315

A good example of PDF analysis utilized in probing the local structure is given in Malavasi ' s work 318 where the phase transition of the fast oxide-ion conductor La2Mo2O9 was investigated. Despite the long-range structure change from α (monoclinic) to β (cubic) phase, no substantial change was observed in the local structures, which remained monoclinic. Hence, a peculiar phase-transition mechanism of long-range ordering to dynamic, short-range ordering, preserving the local monoclinic structure, was proposed. 31,316,319 A similar case was also reported for Zr 0.8 Sc0.2 -x Y x O1.9 . 315 Other local information that is derivable from PDF /uniFB01 tting includes site splitting, 310,311 site disorder, 31,320 the location of interstitial atoms, 313,321 the defect structure, 317 and the defect association which is often observed in oxide-ion conductors. 315,322,323 In oxygen-excess conductor La2NiO4+ δ , 310 when the /uniFB01 tting quality indicator of the PDF ( R w) was plotted against the position of one of the oxygen atoms (O4), two local minima were observed (one at x ∼ 0.13 and the other at x ∼ 0.26), as shown in Figure 20b. This /uniFB01 nding suggests that the apical O4 site of NiO 6 octahedra splits into two sites upon the introduction of excess oxygen atoms.

Deviation from long-range structure was also observed in Na conductors. 324,325 Krauskopf et al. 324 investigated local structural e /uniFB00 ects on the ion conduction properties of ' cubic ' and tetragonal Na 3 PS4. The results indicate that although ' c ' -Na3PS4 displays an averaged cubic structure, the local structure remains analogous to that of the tetragonal phase. The higher ionic conductivity of the ' cubic ' phase was attributed to the high defect concentration resulting from the complex local structures. These results indicate that complex defects (such as the coexistence of low-symmetry and high-symmetry domains) may be present in a wide range of fast ionic conductors, motivating a systematic study of fast ionic conductors using local probes such as PDF and NMR.

3.2.2.2. Nuclear Magnetic Resonance. The sensitivity of NMRtowardlocal, atomic-scale structure enables its application in the investigations of positional or compositional disorder in solid materials. The six most important NMR interactions are Zeeman interaction, paramagnetic, chemical shift, quadrupolar, dipolar, and J -coupling interactions. We discuss the latter four, which are most relevant to the local structure of fast ionic conductors, in the following paragraph.

Because of orbiting electrons around the nucleus, an e /uniFB00 ective magnetic /uniFB01 eld B ′ is generated proportional to the external /uniFB01 eld B 0 , and thus,

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

Figure 21. (a) Schematic illustration of inequivalent sites in the proton conductor CsH2PO4. Reproduced with permission from ref 293. Copyright 2015 American Chemical Society. (b) In the /uniFB02 uorine conductor Ba 0.5 Ca0.5 F2, F atoms form di /uniFB00 erent coordination environments with Ca and Ba. The proportion of F in each coordination environment was derived from NMR spectra deconvolution. Reproduced with permission from ref 328. Copyright 2011 American Chemical Society. (c) Di /uniFB00 erent P -S coordination environments determined by Raman spectroscopy and 31 P MAS NMR for Li 2 S:P2 S5 . The proportions of P in di /uniFB00 erent coordination environments were derived from integrated areas of deconvoluted peaks. Reproduced with permission from ref 319. Copyright 2017 Royal Society of Chemistry.

Here σ is a /uniFB01 eld-independent chemical shielding tensor, which is related to the local electronic environment surrounding the target nucleus.

Chemically inequivalent sites possessing di /uniFB00 erent bond types, bond lengths, bond angles, neighboring atoms, and coordination environments can be distinguished from chemical shift interactions. These features of NMR chemical shifts have enabled a considerable amount of research investigating the bonding conditions 100,293,319,326,327 and coordination environments 50,196,328 of ionic conductors.

̈

For example, in the proton conductor CsH 2PO4, H, O, and P atoms are bonded with neighboring atoms in distinct ways (Figure 21a), i.e., chemically inequivalent sites. Distinguishing these sites is essential to attain a comprehensive understanding of the correlation between rotational PO4 anion groups and hopping protons. There are four types of oxygen atoms in CsH2PO4. O1 is the apical oxygen atom of the PO 4 tetrahedron. O2 and O3 are basal oxygen atoms of the PO 4 group; however, O3 is bonded with H through hydrogen bonds. O3 atoms are further classi /uniFB01 ed into acceptor and donor O3 atoms, denoted as O3 a and O3 d , respectively, according to the hydrogen bonds. Later, along with other ion transport measurements, PO 4 group rotation around the P -O1 axis was shown to be easier than that around the P -O2 and P -O3 axes, contributing to proton transport. 293 Du vel et al. 328 (Figure 21b for the oxide-ion conductor Ba0.5 Ca0.5 F2) and Dietrich et al. 319 (Figure 21c for Liion conductor Li2S -P2S5) utilized the NMR technique in similar ways, where various coordination environments were able to be distinguished and semiquantitative analysis of the contribution from individual peaks could be performed. On the basis of such structural information, mobile ion migration and lattice motion in ionic conductors can be distinguished.

Quadrupolar interactions occur with nuclei with spin quantum number I &gt; 1/2 and/or with asymmetric charge distribution. Quadrupolar interaction, on one hand, is very sensitive to local symmetry, as di /uniFB00 erent coordination environments can yield very di /uniFB00 erent quadrupolar coupling constants ( C Q), related to structure deviation from cubic symmetry, and asymmetry factors ( η ), related to deviation from axial symmetry. These parameters were investigated in several works on lithium conductors, revealing various distortion existing in polyhedra. 329 -334 In the oxide-ion conductor Bi 1 -x Sr x VO4 -0.5 x , 134 di /uniFB00 erent coordination environments formed with various extents of Sr substitution in the BiVO 4 structure led to distinct resonances in NMR spectra because of quadrupolar interactions (Figure 22). NMR spectra of the VO x group in various coordination environments, i.e., the V2O7 group with two neighboring Sr atoms and /uniFB01 ve or six neighboring Bi atoms, VO 4 tetrahedra neighboring with two Sr atoms and six Bi atoms, with one Sr atom and seven Bi atoms, and surrounded by eight Bi atoms, were calculated. Very di /uniFB00 erent chemical shifts ( δ ISO ) of each environment and distinct quadrupolar coupling constants ( | C Q | ) were observed. However, NMR peak broadening caused by quadrupolar interaction can be very large, which may mask other important NMR interactions such as chemical shifts, J -coupling and dipolar coupling, making unambiguous analysis of NMR spectra challenging.

J -Coupling originates from indirect coupling through bonds and is relatively less studied in the investigations of ionic conductors. In some proton conductors (e.g., CsH2PO4), J -

Figure 22. Calculated 51 V isotropic chemical shift along with quadrupolar coupling constants ( | C Q | ) of VO x group for various coordination environments. Reproduced with permission from ref 134. Copyright 2018 Springer Nature.

coupling has been observed in doublet resonances due to 31 P -1 H J -coupling e /uniFB00 ects. 326

Dipolar interactions arise from interactions of local /uniFB01 elds of nuclei and are not restricted in chemical bonds. The strength of dipolar interactions is a /uniFB00 ected by interatomic distance, dipole orientation, and atomic motion. For a given structure, atomic motion mainly alters the strength of dipolar interactions. With increasing mobility of the probed nucleus, narrowing of the line width due to reduced dipolar interactions is often observed. 55,100,196,289,335 -340 Dynamic information including the onset temperature of ion motion and the ion jump frequency for ionic conductors can be derived. 100,289,335 -338

3.2.2.3. X-ray Absorption Spectroscopy. Compared with PDF analysis, X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) is an element-speci /uniFB01 c technique. In typical XAS measurements, the X-ray energy is tuned close to the absorption edge of the probed element. The absorption coe /uniFB03 cients of the sample are then measured as a function of the X-ray energy. The absorption coe /uniFB03 cient increases substantially at the absorption edge of the element. The absorption edge position contains information regarding the oxidation state of the element. The features before (pre-edge) and after (extended edge) the absorption edge re /uniFB02 ect the local structure surrounding the probed element. In particular, analysis of the extended edge region, often termed extended X-ray absorption /uniFB01 ne structure (EXAFS) analysis, can be facilitated by small-box modeling (similar to Rietveld re /uniFB01 nement). A structural model is re /uniFB01 ned against the EXAFS data, yielding important local structural information, such as bond distances, the coordination number, and the Debye -Waller factor (similar to the thermal parameter in XRD). EXAFS amplitudes in k space decay exponentially as exp( -2 k 2 ε αβ 2 ), where ε αβ is the mean square deviation from R αβ ( R αβ is the mean pair distance of atoms α and β ). ε αβ is often large at elevated temperatures or in materials with a large degree of disorder. Therefore, the decay can be easily observed in fast ionic conductors, which are usually rich in disorder. In addition, ε αβ is even larger for more distant shells from the central atom. All these facts suggest that only nearest-neighbor correlations can be reliably extracted from EXAFS analysis.

XAS has been adopted to extract local structural information, such as the site occupancy, local environment of mobile ions, and conduction pathway before and after superionic phase transition. 341 -343 Boyce et al. 341 resolved the silver ion transport mechanism of AgI from EXAFS results. Ag + ions were proved to occupy tetrahedral sites that are displaced from the tetrahedral center with three I neighbors at r = 2.78 Å and one I neighbor at 2.93 Å. In addition, the amplitude of EXAFS peaks was reduced by 25% to achieve perfect /uniFB01 tting of the spectrum ( R = 0.09), indicating that only 75% of Ag atoms contribute to EXAFS features, as shown in Figure 23a. In the Strock model, which

̈

Reproduced with permission from ref 341. Copyright 1977 American Physical Society.

̈

assumes that two Ag + ions are homogeneously distributed in the bcc lattice formed by I atoms, a poor /uniFB01 tting ( R = 0.60) was achieved (Figure 23b). Upon /uniFB01 tting using the Bu hrer -Ha lg model (Figure 23c), where Ag ions occupy 24 g tetrahedral sites with two I atoms at r = 2.71 Å and two I atoms at r = 2.96 Å, the quality of /uniFB01 tting was similarly poor ( R =0.50). Hence, the model in which only 75% of Ag atoms contribute to the EXAFS feature best to /uniFB01 t the data. On the basis of this proportion, the ratio of Ag residence time τ R and /uniFB02 ight time τ F , i.e., τ R / τ F , was derived to be 3.0 ± 0.6 for AgI at 198 ° C, indicating a jump-di /uniFB00 usion model of Ag ion transport. In another study of a silver conductor α -RbAg4I5, Stutius et al. 344 investigated the variation of shortrange repulsive Ag -Ag correlation under varying temperatures. Onthe basis of the assumption that uncorrelated Ag atoms have a rather broad distribution of Ag -Ag distances and do not contribute signi /uniFB01 cantly to the EXAFS peak intensity, the decreasing amplitude of the Ag -Ag pair with increasing temperature was attributed to weaker correlations between Ag ions at higher temperatures. Similar correlation e /uniFB00 ects were also observed in Cu conductors (e.g., α -CuI). 345

Figure 24. (a) State-of-the-art (S)TEM techniques and their applications in ion transport research. Low-angle annular dark/uniFB01 eld (LAADF) is a STEM method that uses a annular dark/uniFB01 eld (ADF) detector to receive di /uniFB00 racted or inelastically scattered electrons at low to medium angles (25 to 60 mrad). For Cu + ion conductors (e.g., CuCl, CuBr, and CuI), Boyce et al. 342,343 derived the preferred sites of mobile ions and even the preferred di /uniFB00 usion pathways based on a near-neighbor pair correlation function. The signi /uniFB01 cant occupancies at intermediate trigonal sites at high temperature indicate migration in ⟨ 110 ⟩ directions in bcc lattices ( α -AgI, α -CuBr), whereas in fcc materials ( α -CuI, γ -cuprous halides), preferred occupancies at octahedral positions indicate ion transport in ⟨ 111 ⟩ directions through faces shared by tetrahedra and octahedra. Moreover, in these works, the structural essence of the superionic phase transition of these materials was discussed based on XAS data obtained at various temperatures.

3.2.3. Point Defects. The nature and concentration of point defects play crucial role in ion transport. The point defect type a /uniFB00 ects the ion jump mechanism and therefore the jump distance, as illustrated in Figure 2. The defect concentration is usually related to the mobile ion concentration. In this section, we describe how the defect type and concentration are determined by experimental techniques. In addition, local or short-range structure, such as ' disordered ' domains, vacancies, and distortion of the lattice structure, signi /uniFB01 cantly a /uniFB00 ect the di /uniFB00 usion of ions in fast ion conductors. 346

3.2.3.1. Di /uniFB00 use Scattering. Although the short-range defects are usually characterized by electron microscopes, di /uniFB00 use scattering measurement has been extensively used since the 1970s. Di /uniFB00 use scattering is one of two components of total scattering. The other component is Bragg scattering, which measures the long-range, time-averaged component. Di /uniFB00 use scattering measurement is usually designed to rapidly measure a large portion of reciprocal space, usually using single-crystal samples.

Defects in materials, such as stacking faults, substitutions, magnetic disturbances, movement of ions, and lattice relaxation, can be observed in di /uniFB00 use scattering. We note that electrons, Xrays, and neutrons can all be used for di /uniFB00 use scattering measurements. In conventional Bragg di /uniFB00 raction, such signals are considered to be interference, which increases the background signal and reduces the accuracy of Bragg intensity extraction. However, for the characterization of disordered materials, di /uniFB00 use scattering is rather useful. 185 For instance, Pro /uniFB00 en et al. 347 observed two types of defects in oxide-ion conductor calcium-stabilized zirconia:microdomains based on one single oxygen vacancy with relaxed neighboring ions and microdomains based on a pair of oxygen vacancies along ⟨ 111 ⟩ . In another study on oxide-ion conductor yttria-stabilized zirconia, the di /uniFB00 use scattering rings was ascribed to shortrange ordering of oxygen vacancies like that in the pyrochlore structure, which can be depicted as a superstructure of the /uniFB02 uorite type on account of ordering of the anion vacancies forming zigzag chains along the [110] direction. 348

3.2.3.2. Scanning Transmission Electron Microscopy. Defects in materials can be visualized using scanning transmission electron microscopies (STEM). The spatial resolution of state-of-the-art STEM has reached beyond 0.5 Å by equipping the microscopes with a spherical aberration corrector, and the energy resolution has reached down to 10 meV. A schematic diagram is presented in Figure 24a. 349

Two techniques in the imaging mode of STEM are capable of producing atomic-scale mapping of materials, namely, highangle annular dark/uniFB01 eld (HAADF) imaging and annular bright/uniFB01 eld (ABF) imaging. In addition, two other analytical tools can also be used in STEM: (i) electron energy loss spectroscopy (EELS) to analyze the energy distribution of transmitted electrons and (ii) X-ray energy dispersion spectroscopy (XEDS) to identify the characteristic X-rays of elements produced by the interaction between the probed sample and electron beam. Combining these techniques, the material chemical information can be studied at the atomic level, 350 identifying the defect type. In perovskite lanthanum lithium titanate (LLTO), Gao et al. 351 used STEM and EELS and found local clustering of the A-site (Wycko /uniFB00 3 b ) vacancies resulting in aggregation of Li atoms, as shown in Figure 24b. On the basis of these results, they proposed

Figure 25. (a) From top down to bottom: the three lines represent 19 F MAS NMR spectra for nanocrystalline CaF2 with crystallite sizes of ∼ 25 and 50 nm and bulk CaF2, respectively. The inset shows the region between -140 and -120 ppm for these samples, magni /uniFB01 ed by 20 × . Reproduced with permission from ref 353. Copyright 2010 American Chemical Society. (b) Charge redistribution in Y-resonant XPS for BaCe 0.8 Y0.2O3 -δ in UHV (dry) and in water vapor with p (H2O)=100mTorr(wet),revealing the charge redistribution in electronic structure. The di /uniFB00 erence in wet and dry samples is shown in red area. Reproduced with permission from ref 217. Copyright 2013 American Chemical Society.

that the Li-ion migration pathway changes from 2D in Li-poor LLTOto 3D in Li-rich LLTO, attributing to the associated local clustering of A-site vacancies and the conglomeration of Li atoms.

In addition, it was revealed using STEM that the modulated crystal structure in Li-ion conductor La (1 -x )/3 Li x NbO3 (LLNO) was derived from high La and O vacancy concentrations. 308 Such structure modulation is absent in La0.33NbO3 (LNO), as shown in Figure 24c. However, ion mobility was restricted by the structure modulation because of deeper local potential wells.

Although an atomic-resolution defect map can be obtained using STEM, it remains di /uniFB03 cult to conclude whether all the defects are carriers and to elucidate the relation between defects and carriers. Therefore, other techniques are necessary to further investigate how defects a /uniFB00 ect ion transport.

3.2.3.3. Nuclear Magnetic Resonance. NMR spectroscopy can be employed to investigate point defects because di /uniFB00 erent atoms, atomic groups, and defect structure show resonances at di /uniFB00 erent shifts with di /uniFB00 erent NMR interactions. From magicangle spinning NMR (MAS NMR) and Fourier Transform (FT)-Raman analysis, HPO4 2 -was con /uniFB01 rmed to be the dominate defect in proton conductor Sr-doped LaPO4 (Sr = 0 -7 mol%). 352 The concentration of point defects can be identi /uniFB01 ed by comparing the peak area of point defects with that of other resonances corresponding to lattice sites. For example, 19 F fast MAS NMR spectra of nanocrystalline CaF2 (diameter ∼ 25 nm) revealed the presence of ∼ 0.55% of F -ions in interstitial sites, as observed in Figure 25a. The resonance located at approximately -132 ppm was attributed to interstitial F -, which was observed to be highly mobile at elevated temperatures and to undergo rapid site exchange with regular /uniFB02 uorine sites in the lattice via hopping. An increased concentration of these defects formed in nanocrystalline CaF 2 enhances the /uniFB02 uorine ionic conductivity by nearly 3 orders of magnitude compared with that of bulk CaF2. 353 In oxide-ion conductors, by employing multinuclear variable-temperature solid-state NMR ( 17 O, 51 V, and 31 P NMR), Dunstan et al. observed that a high dopant concentration in /uniFB02 uorite-type bismuth vanadate and phosphate solid electrolytes will disrupt the 3D conduction network of oxide ions and that the coordination /uniFB02 exibility will also a /uniFB00 ect the oxide-ion mobility. 354

3.2.3.4. Other Methods. Other methods are also available to investigate point defects and improve our understanding of how point defects a /uniFB00 ect ion transport. 324 In the chromium K-edge XAS study of (Na0.5 Bi 0.5TiO3)(1 -x )(BiCrO3) x (NBT) perovskite solid solution, Selvamani et al. 355 observed no strong pre-edge peak, which implies that the chromium ion is not in the +6 state. They concluded that chromium sits at the octahedral site of NBT with +3 or +5 valence state, which generates O or Bi/Na vacancies in NBT, respectively. Combining impedance experiments and computational simulation, they proposed that the ionic conductivity is contributed by oxygen vacancies generated by the incorporation of +3 chromium.

For proton conductors, a conventional approach to measure the defect concentration is using thermogravimetric analysis, e.g., to measure the weight gain of dry sample under humid conditions, assuming that the entire weight gain is due to hydration. 46 The defect concentration of other ions can be determined using X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) by measuring the valence state of elements in the sample. Combining the results with the law of charge conservation, the defect concentration can be calculated indirectly. Furthermore, synchrotron-based ambient-pressure XPS can provide element-speci /uniFB01 c information on the electronic structure of materials. In proton conductor BaCe 0.8 Y0.2O3 -δ , the O, Ce, and Y core level as well as resonant valence band spectra revealed that at the onset temperature of proton conductivity the oxygen vacancies are /uniFB01 lled upon water exposure, along with a charge redistribution among lattice O, Ce, and Y, as illustrated in Figure 25b. 217

3.2.4. Lattice Dynamics. Atoms in solids are always oscillating around their equilibrium positions at any temperature above absolute zero. The theory to describe such oscillations is often referred to as lattice dynamics. Optical spectroscopy, such as infrared (IR) and Raman, inelastic neutron scattering (INS), and ultrasonic speed of sound measurements (USOS) have been employed to characterize the frequency of lattice vibration modes. INS is an e /uniFB00 ective technique to study the total phonon density of state (pDOS), while the IR and Raman probe the optical phonon branch and the USOS is used to study acoustic phonon branch. The lattice vibrations may enhance or hinder the di /uniFB00 usion of ions. It has also been demonstrated recently that the ion migration barrier is lower for softer lattices. 29 -31 Modulating the lattice dynamics is considered as an emerging strategy for the discovery of new ionic conductors with enhanced conductivity and stability. Below, we introduce the

Figure 26. (a) The seven stoichiometric LISICONs and Li4GeO4 ( Cmcm ) comparison in measured activation energy and measured (total) band center, respectively. Reproduced with permission from ref 29. Copyright 2018 Royal Society of Chemistry. (b) INS spectrum at T ≤ 10 K of 50In/BZO recorded by two instruments, TOSCA (I) and MERLIN (II). (III) I ( Q , ω ) maps of 50In/BZO. Reproduced with permission from ref 356. Copyright 2019 Royal Society of Chemistry.

Figure 27. (a) Change of wavenumber (I) and relative integrated intensity (II) of the four Gaussian bands A -D with the In content x increasing in BaInxZr1 -x O3 -x /2 . Reproduced with permission from ref 203. Copyright 2005 American Physical Society. (b) Raman frequency shifts in BaCe0.8Y0.2O3 -δ as a function of pressure. Reproduced with permission from ref 154. Copyright 2011 American Chemical Society.

techniques to characterize lattice dynamics and discuss its correlation with ion transport for Li-ion, Na-ion, oxide-ion and proton conductors.

3.2.4.1. Inelastic Neutron Scattering. Inelastic neutron scattering (INS) is a method used to study the atomic or molecular vibrations and other crystal /uniFB01 eld excitations. Neutron scattering is inelastic when energy and momentum exchange occur in the collision event between a neutron and the sample. INS measures the scattering function S ( Q , E ) as a function of momentum transfer Q and energy transfer E , which reveals the phonon density of states (pDOS).

Combined with computational methods, INS can elaborate the e /uniFB00 ects of lattice dynamics on the ion mobility and material stability of ionic conductors. For instance, Paulus et al. 138 claimed that in the oxide-ion conductor SrFeO 3 -x , the reduction of Fe -O -Fe bond strength in the FeO6 octahedra lead to enhanced and phonon-assisted oxygen di /uniFB00 usion, triggered by the dynamical switching of FeO 4 tetrahedral chains between two di /uniFB00 erent directions. Such a dynamically triggered phenomenon helps the oxide ions to easily migrate into the open vacancy channels. For lithium-ion conductors, combining INS and DFT, Muy et al. 29 concluded that the fast Li-ion migration of LISICONs is linked with low-lithium phonon band centers and that their high stability is associated with high anion band centers, as illustrated in Figure 26a.

Analyses of the INS signal provides unique information that can be used to characterize the proton sites because the hydrogen bond strength is a /uniFB00 ected by protons and the relative occupation of the proton sites. In the INS spectra of the proton conductor 50% In-doped BaZrO3 (50In/BZO) 356 in Figure 26b, the weak and broad features centered at 200, 420, 500, and 650 meV suggest O -H bending with some di /uniFB00 erent O -H distances, revealing several distinguishable proton sites in the crystal structure.

3.2.4.2. Optical Spectroscopy. The optical phonon mode and molecular vibration modes of materials can also be characterized using optical spectroscopy (Raman and IR spectroscopy). The molecular vibration, phonons, or other excitations will interact with the incident light, resulting in an energy change of the incident photons. This shift in energy can

be used to reveal the frequency of vibrational modes in the investigated material. 357 The Raman-active vibrations must change the polarizability α of the electron cloud, and IR-active vibrations must change the dipole moment μ of the molecule. Therefore, IR and Raman are considered as complementary techniques. In the near-infrared to near-ultraviolet range, the two techniques are commonly accessible in laboratories and require a much smaller sample volume compared with neutron experiments.

IR and Raman spectroscopy have been applied to study the correlation between bond vibration information and ion motion. 154 In proton conductor BaIn x Zr1 -x O3 -x /2 , Karlsson et al. 203 observed a strong red-shift of the O -H stretching band (Gaussian bands A) in the IR spectrum with increasing In content (Figure 27a), indicating that protons tilt toward adjacent oxygen to form hydrogen bonds. However, the formation of hydrogen bonds lowers the reorientation mobility of protons, thereby hinder the long-range migration.

For tuning the lattice dynamics, in addition to the change in material stoichiometry, mechanical pressure is considered an alternative approach that does not a /uniFB00 ect the material chemistry or processing. By applying compressive strain to the proton conductor BaCe0.8 Y0.2O3 -δ , a strong pressure-dependent increase in the B1 g and B3 g Raman modes (Ce -O stretching mode) was reported, as illustrated in Figure 27b, which implies anisotropic lattice hardening and con /uniFB01 nement in proton transport. 154

3.2.4.3. Ultrasonic Speed of Sound. Ultrasonic speed of sound (USOS) measurement is used to probe lattice sti /uniFB00 ness, Debye frequencies, and pDOS for acoustic phonons. When the ultrasonic signal is applied to one end of the sample, the signal is rapidly transmitted inside, and rebound when the ultrasonic wave reaches the other end of the sample. When the detector receives the backscattered signal, the signal transmitted distance is 2 d ( d is the thickness of the sample). Then, the speed of sound of the transverse waves ( v t ) and longitudinal waves ( v l ) can be calculated using the following equations:

where Δ t and Δ l are the time interval between the incident and backscattered transverse and longitudinal waves, respectively. The mean speed of sound v mean, Debye temperature Θ D, and Debye frequency v D can be obtained using eq 18 -20

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->
