where V represent the average volume per atom. The Debye temperature and Debye frequency both re /uniFB02 ect the vibration frequency of phonons in the materials and are directly related to the mean speed of sound. Stronger bonding interactions (i.e., shorter bond lengths) cause an increased mean speed of sound

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

in solids because of higher vibration frequency of phonons. 358 The mean speed of sound is thus a good indicator of phonon properties and lattice sti /uniFB00 ness. For example, a high mean speed of sound is associated with a high average bond strength, high Debye frequency, and high Debye temperature, all of which indicate harder and sti /uniFB00 er lattices.

The cation-substituted Li-ion conductor Li10Ge1 -x Sn x P2S12 exhibits a higher activation barrier in a softer lattice. With increasing Sn 4+ , although the lattice becomes softer, the bottleneck along the di /uniFB00 usion path in the z -direction is tightened and the local ionic bonding between Li + and S 2 -becomes stronger, leading to a higher activation barrier. 33

The underlying correlation between lattice sti /uniFB00 ness and ion transport is complex. By increasing the polarizability of the anion sublattice in Li 6 PS5X (X = Cl, Br, I) from Cl to I, the speed of sound decreases, indicating weaker bonds and lower lattice sti /uniFB00 ness. 31 The softer lattice leads to a decrease in the activation barrier; however, the prefactor is also reduced. Because of the prefactor conundrum and other complex structural factors, Li conductivity in Li6PS5 -x Se x Br (0 ≤ x ≤ 1) shows no obvious improvement when its lattice becomes softer. 320 Softening of the lattice vibration frequency is also correlated with sodium-ion transport. 32

## 3.3. Comparisons and Limitations of Experimental Techniques

Each technique that probes ion transport processes is sensitive to a certain range of time scales. QENS (section 3.1.1) is a favorable tool to investigate proton motion and has been applied to probe O and Li, and potentially to Na, although much longer data collection time is expected for Li and Na conductors. μ + SR (section 3.1.1) requires much smaller sample size compared to QENS and is therefore often applied to study Li- and Na-ion transport and can be potentially implemented to investigate other types of ions. NMR relaxometry (section 3.1.1), 2D NMR (section 3.1.1), and PFG NMR (section 3.1.2) can reveal ion dynamic processes at various time scales and will remain valuable for studies of ion transport mechanisms. However, to reveal the exact nature of ion transport processes, complementary techniques such as QENS or DFT modeling are most likely needed. For long-range ion di /uniFB00 usion, impedance spectroscopy (section 3.1.2) has been universally applied to analyze ion conductors of di /uniFB00 erent species, although care should be taken to separate grain and grain boundary contributions. In this regard, measurements with microelectrodes can be useful. The tracer di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient is an important yet often overlooked parameter (section 3.1.2). The task of introducing an isotope to the material will remain a challenge. Once the isotope is introduced to the system, we expect that spatially resolved techniques, such as MRI and NDP, in addition to TOF-SIMS, can be used to determine the spatial distribution of isotopes, thus opening up alternative means to measure tracer di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cients. It is worth to clarify that unlike NMR relaxometry and 2D NMR which measure random di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient D r , PFG NMR characterizes the tracer di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient D * .

Di /uniFB00 raction techniques have been widely applied in studying the long-range crystal structure of materials for decades. Depending on the light source, di /uniFB00 raction techniques primarily consist of XRD, ND, and ED (section 3.2.1). With detailed re /uniFB01 nement of XRD and ND patterns, the positions of mobile ions in the structure and the corresponding site occupancies can be determined. ND is sensitive to H, Li, and O and is thus a powerful tool to study proton, Li-ion, and oxide-ion conductors.

Figure 28. Schematic of the combined approach of INS, Raman/IR, speed of sound (SOS), and EIS for monitoring the changing lattice dynamics in ionic conductors. Δ H mig is enthalpy of migration (activation energy E a ), and υ D is Debye frequency. Reproduced with permission from ref 30. Copyright 2018 American Chemical Society.

Li is suggested to be isotope enriched for neutron di /uniFB00 raction using 7 Li because of the large absorption and incoherent scattering of 6 Li.

The local structure can be probed using PDF analysis, XAS, and NMR spectroscopy (section 3.2.2). Atomic pair distances, polyhedral shape, size, and local distortion can be extracted from PDF data. XAS, however, can be considered as selected histograms of the PDF data. When hard X-rays are employed, elements that are detectable in general start with the /uniFB01 rst period of transition metals, i.e., titanium. By using soft X-rays, however, light elements such as O and F can be probed, enabling direct probing of such mobile ions. NMR spectroscopy is another versatile technique that can provide information on the local structure of materials. With appropriate deconvolution of peaks, chemically inequivalent sites, the coordination environment (defects included), jump distances, and the orientations of polyanions can be probed.

Understanding the nature of defects in fast ionic conductors is extremely important but di /uniFB03 cult (section 3.2.3). Di /uniFB00 use scattering has been used to identify the defect nature, e.g. vacancies. Using STEM, spatially resolved spectroscopy analysis at the atomic scale is possible. This method can provide valuable information regarding defect (or carrier) type but cannot quantify the defect concentration. XPS can be used to identify the defect type and estimate the composition of a sample but is only sensitive to the /uniFB01 rst few nanometers of the particle surface, lacking sensitivity to the bulk of the material. NMR spectroscopy can be an extremely useful tool to identify defect type and concentration, provided that enough defects are present in the sample. On the basis of the proposed structure, NMR parameters can be simulated using DFT, leading to precise assignment of NMR resonances.

Phonons, either acoustic or optical, have been recently shown to couple with ion transport to a great extent (section 3.2.4). When measuring the pDOS, inelastic neutron scattering provides a sum over all phonon modes. Nevertheless, the assignment of di /uniFB00 erent bands is crucial, as recently shown by Muy et al., who reported that lithium migration is correlated to the lithium phonon band center. 29 USOS can be used to probe the acoustic phonons and determine the Debye frequencies/

temperature. IR and Raman spectroscopy are exclusively used to monitor the chemistry-induced changes in the optical phonon modes. Similar to INS, accurate assignment of IR and Raman bands are critical to identify the exact phonon modes responsible for superionic conduction. A combination of di /uniFB00 erent approaches is therefore necessary to obtain a complete phonon dispersion spectrum. Recently, pDOS of sodium thiophosphate has been depicted by Zeier and co-workers, 30 combining Raman, EIS, SOS, and INS, making an important /uniFB01 rst step toward a comprehensive characterization of phonon dispersion spectrum in ionic conductors. Figure 28 represents a good summary of the scope these techniques can cover.

## 4. COMPUTATIONAL TECHNIQUES

In this section, we review computational methods used to study ion di /uniFB00 usion and transport in crystalline structures. We discuss the computation of defects and dopants (section 4.1), di /uniFB00 usion mechanisms (section 4.2, section 4.3), and the stability of structures (section 4.4). We then compare the strengths and limitations of current computational techniques (section 4.5).

## 4.1. Formation of Mobile Carriers

Because ionic di /uniFB00 usion in solids is mediated by the hopping of atoms in the crystal lattice, a high concentration of mobile-ion carriers, often in the form of vacancies and interstitials, is crucial to achieve high ionic conductivity. First-principles computation methods have been established for determining the formation energy of point defects, such as vacancy, self-interstitial, dopant, or substitutional atoms in a material, and the e /uniFB00 ects of these defects on the electronic structure and ion transport. 13,359 -362

The formation energy of a defect in a crystal solid is determined by the energy di /uniFB00 erence between the crystal model with the defect and the perfect defect-free crystal plus a reference energy (i.e., chemical potential) of the defect species, which is dependent on the thermodynamic conditions of the materials. 363 -366 Speci /uniFB01 cally, the formation energy E f [ Xq ] of the crystal with the defect Xq with charge q can be calculated as

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

Figure 29. (a) Predicted formation energies and (b) concentrations of various point defects in Li 2 CO3 as a function of Li chemical potential. Reproduced with permission from ref 367. Copyright 2012 American Chemical Society. (c) Decomposition energies for doped Na0.5Bi0.5TiO3 compositions with four types of cation substitution at 1500 K. Reproduced with permission from ref 79. Copyright 2015 PCCP Owner Societies.

where E 0 is the energy of the perfect crystal without the defect, ni is the number of atoms of species i that have been added or removed from the crystal to form the defect, and μ i is the chemical potential of the species i . The formation of defects with a nonzero charge q can occur in nonmetallic materials such as ionic solids or semiconductors. The formation of charged defects involves the addition or removal of electrons from the material, and the reference energy of these electrons should be considered and is often given by the Fermi energy /uni03F5 F of the materials at the given stoichiometry and thermodynamic conditions. 363 -370 The chemical potential μ i is a reference energy of the atom species involved in creating the defect 363 -366 and is dependent on thermodynamic variables, e.g., pressure, temperature, concentration, and applied voltage. Therefore, the selection of the values of μ i in eq 21 should re /uniFB02 ect the physical conditions of the materials. For example, the formation of an oxygen vacancy or oxygen interstitial within oxide-ion conductors should be referenced to the partial pressure and temperature of gaseous oxygen under operational conditions, as oxygen ions in the materials exchange with the external gaseous environment. 371 -373 In solid electrolyte or electrode materials for Li-ion batteries, the chemical potential of Li μ Li is related to the applied potential and can be given by

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

where ϕ is the applied potential referenced to Li metal and μ Li 0 is the chemical potential of Li metal. For intrinsic defects of the existing elements in the material, the range of chemical potentials is often set to the stable range of μ i in the particular material phase, which can be determined from grand canonical phase diagrams. 374 -376

For many ion conductors that are mediated by the migration of intrinsic defects, the formation energy of these mobile-carrier defects should be considered, in addition to the migration energy barrier, as a part of the total activation energy of the Arrhenius relation for ion transport. 377 -379 First-principles computation can be used to evaluate the formation energy of di /uniFB00 erent types of defects within a given structure and to determine the dominant mobile carriers for ion di /uniFB00 usion by comparing various defects. For example, in a computational study on lithium di /uniFB00 usion within the solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI), Shi et al. calculated the formation energy and defect concentration for a range of point defects in Li2CO3 (Figure 29a,b), a major component of the SEI, including Li interstitials (Li i , Li i + ), Li vacancies ( Vi , Vi -), and Frenkel pairs (Li FP , Li FP + , Li FP -). 10 The authors observed that positive Li interstitials (Li i + ) had the lowest formation energy in the range of voltages from the potential of Li metal to the voltage corresponding to SEI formation and thus argued that Li i + interstitials are the dominant charge carrier in Li 2 CO3. With this knowledge of the dominant mobile carrier, the Li-ion transport mechanism in Li2CO3 and the SEI was further evaluated by computation, as discussed in section 4.2.

Given that the concentration of intrinsic defects is often limited in pristine materials, doping and substitution have been widely employed to create carriers for improving the ion transport properties of materials. By substituting aliovalent ions in the host, charge-compensating defects of the desired mobile species can be created. For example, to create Li interstitials in NASICON-structured LiTi2(PO4)3, Al 3+ is substituted for Ti 4+ to i nduce extra Li + i nto the structure, f orming Li1+ x Al x Ti2 -x (PO4)3 with high ionic conductivity. 181,380 Although basic heuristic rules and experience with elementary

Figure 30. NEB evaluation of ion migration in (a) spinel Mn 2 O4 and (b) olivine FePO 4 at the high vacancy limit (solid line) and dilute vacancy limit (dotted line). (c) Preferred migration pathway of an intercalating ion (red atoms) in a spinel oxide structure (O: yellow atoms), which migrates from a low-energy tetrahedral site ( E s ) into a neighboring octahedral site ( E i ) with some activation energy ( E a ) and /uniFB01 nally to a symmetric tetrahedral site. (d) Preferred migration pathway of an intercalating ion in an olivine-structured oxide. Reproduced with permission from ref 144. Copyright 2015 American Chemical Society.

substitution are often relied upon to guide such substitutions, /uniFB01 rst-principles computation can be used to identify potential doping elements that can be stably substituted into the material and to elucidate the e /uniFB00 ect of dopants on the di /uniFB00 usion behavior (section 4.2). For example, a computational study by He et al. 79 on the oxide-ion conductor Na0.5 Bi 0.5TiO3 (NBT) explored various types of aliovalent cation substitution for A-site Bi 3+ and B-site Ti 4+ in the perovskite structure. Because oxygen ion conduction in the perovskite-structured Na0.5 Bi 0.5TiO3 was mediated by oxygen vacancies, 79,381 a wide range of aliovalent cations with lower valences than Bi 3+ or Ti 4+ , which increase the oxygen vacancy concentration, were computationally evaluated to identify the most energetically favorable dopants (Figure 29c). Multiple dopants with low energy predicted by computation such as Zn, Fe, and Mg on B-sites have been experimentally reported. 79,381 -384 In addition, a number of new aliovalent dopants were predicted to yield NBT perovskite structures with higher oxygen vacancy concentration with acceptable phase stability. Compared with B-site substitution, A-site substitution, such as the substitution of K for Bi, was observed to be particularly e /uniFB00 ective in improving the ionic conductivity while maintaining the phase stability. 79,383 In NBT, although dopant elements with lower valence than the original B-site atom, such as Mg 2+ , create oxygen vacancies, these dopants also increase the oxygen migration barrier owing to the binding between the dopant and created vacancy. The dopant trapping phenomenon has been observed in many types of ion conductors. 385 -388 For example, Toyoura et al. observed that doped W sites trapped interstitial O 2 -ions in the W-doped LaNbO4 oxide-ion conductor, and the authors proposed selecting dopants with smaller association energies, such as Mofor Nb sites, as a solution to suppress this trapping e /uniFB00 ect and improve the ionic conductivity. 386 These e /uniFB00 ects of dopants on ion migration can be evaluated using /uniFB01 rst-principles computation methods including NEB calculations (section 4.2) and AIMD simulations (section 4.3).

## 4.2. Nudged Elastic Band Method

The nudged elastic band (NEB) method implemented in /uniFB01 rstprinciples computation 389,390 is widely used to determine the energy barrier of an ion or atom migrating along a particular pathway in a crystalline structure. A NEB calculation takes the input, such as the initial and /uniFB01 nal migration position of the particular mobile carrier (e.g., Li + vacancy) and an initial guess of the migration pathway and determines the energy landscape along that pathway, the energy of the transition state, and the migration energy barrier ( E a ). In general, a crystalline structure may exhibit multiple ion migration pathways connecting the same or di /uniFB00 erent ion sites, leading to di /uniFB00 erent energy barriers and anisotropic di /uniFB00 usion. The NEB method has contributed signi /uniFB01 cantly to our understanding of atomistic-scale ion migration mechanisms in ion conductors. 143,360 -362,391 -394 For example, Holzwarth and co-workers used NEB calculations to calculate the energy barriers for di /uniFB00 erent defects, such as Li + vacancies and interstitials, migrating along various Li migration pathways in γ -Li3PO4. 395 The authors observed that Li + vacancies migrate in the structure through three pathways starting and ending at low-energy d sites, with barriers between 0.6 and 0.7 eV. For Li + interstitials in γ -Li 3 PO4, direct hopping between interstitial sites was observed to be unfavorable. In contrast, ion hopping via an interstitialcy mechanism, where an interstitial Li-ion replaces a d site lattice ion and that same lattice ion simultaneously migrates to a neighboring interstitial site, was calculated to have the lowest energy barrier of 0.2 -0.35 eV. Given the low migration energy of the interstitialcy mechanism, an increased concentration of Li + interstitials, which can be achieved by preparing a sample with excess Li, is implied to improve the Li + conductivity in γ -Li3PO4. 395

In the study by Shi et al. of Li + di /uniFB00 usion in Li2CO3, 367 a similar comparison between di /uniFB00 erent di /uniFB00 usion mechanisms, such as direct interstitial and correlated interstitialcy hopping, was conducted using the NEB method, following the identi /uniFB01 cation of the dominant charge carrier in the material at a given potential

Figure 31. AIMD simulations for ion conductors. (a) Li-ion (green atoms) trajectory (pink sphere trace) within LGPS crystal structure from AIMD simulation at 900 K. S atoms, PS 4 tetrahedra, and GeS 4 tetrahedra are shown in yellow, light-purple, and dark-purple, respectively. Reproduced with permission from ref 37. Copyright 2011 American Chemical Society. (b) Li-ion probability density from AIMD simulations in the LGPS structure. Inset: Li sites (green) in the S-anion (yellow) framework with arrows indicating the Li-ion migration pathway. Reproduced with permission from ref 80. Copyright 2017 Springer Nature. (c) Distinct part of the van Hove correlation function of Li ions in LGPS, suggesting a strong time-correlation in the Li-ion dynamics. Reproduced with permission from ref 80. Copyright 2017 Springer Nature. (d) Arrhenius plot of Li-ion di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient in Li 10XP2S12 (X = Si, Ge, or Sn) from AIMD simulations. Reproduced with permission from ref 153. Copyright 2013 Royal Society of Chemistry.

on the Li-ion battery electrode. Shi et al. also found that the energy barrier for the concerted migration of an interstitial Liion and a lattice Li-ion was substantially lower than that for the direct interstitial hopping mechanism. This computationally identi /uniFB01 ed atomistic mechanism was used to estimate the pro /uniFB01 le of elemental Li in the SEI, which was further con /uniFB01 rmed by experimental measurements of the isotope ratio 6 Li + / 7 Li + in 7 Li + Li 2 CO3 soaked in electrolyte with 6 Li + species. Skjærvø et al. calculated the NEB barrier for O i migration in YMnO3 and calculated an activation energy between 0.48 and 0.62 eV for interstitialcy-mediated di /uniFB00 usion, whereas simple interstitial di /uniFB00 usion had an extremely unfavorable migration barrier of 6 eV. 371 Their calculated energy barrier agreed well with the activation energy of 0.55 ± 0.21 eV estimated in experiments. 371 The interstitialcy mechanism is a type of concerted migration, which has been shown to be a common ion transport mechanism in many fast ion conductors. 80,87,102,396

NEB calculations can be used to compare the energy barriers for di /uniFB00 usion in di /uniFB00 erent materials. 362 Using NEB calculations, Wolverton and co-workers studied Li i + di /uniFB00 usion via direct interstitial hopping in the cathode coatings SiO2, ZrO2, AlF3, MgO, and Al2O3. 368 Although the majority of materials studied exhibited a high migration energy barrier, amorphous AlF 3 and crystalline SiO 2 had low energy barriers for easier Li + di /uniFB00 usion as coating materials. In addition, the NEB method can be used for screening materials with low migration energy barriers. 385,397 Liu and co-workers calculated the voltage, capacity, and stability of spinel oxides composed of a range of transition metals including V, Ti, and Mn, and performed NEB calculations to determine which structures had the potential to exhibit fast ion di /uniFB00 usion with di /uniFB00 erent intercalating ions including Li + , Ca 2+ , and Mg 2+ . 145 They observed that the migration energy barriers varied signi /uniFB01 cantly with the intercalating ion (Figure 30a,b) and proposed Mn2O4 spinels with Mg 2+ or Ca 2+ insertions as promising cathodes for multivalent-ion batteries. The general features of the NEB ion migration pathways can be associated with the changing oxygen ion environment (Figure 30c,d). 144 A combined computational and experimental study later made use of these basic crystal structural principles to tune and optimize the composition of Mg 2+ spinel solid electrolytes and identi /uniFB01 ed several solids with fast Mg 2+ transport at room temperature. 7

## 4.3. Molecular Dynamics Simulation

Molecular dynamics (MD) simulations model the dynamics of ions in a material with full atomistic details, a subfemtosecond time resolution, and a simulation time up to the nanosecond level. Thus, MD simulations provide information on ion transport on the time scale of subfemto- to nanoseconds. MD simulations can be categorized into classical MD simulations, which are based on interatomic potentials (aka force /uniFB01 elds) for evaluating atomistic forces, and ab initio MD (AIMD) simulations, in which /uniFB01 rst-principles calculations describe interatomic interactions. Because the interatomic potentials for classical MD simulations may not always be available, especially for newly discovered materials, AIMD simulation, which is chemically agnostic in nature, can be readily applied to any material with complex chemistry. For this reason, AIMD simulation has gained popularity in the past few years for studying ion-conducting materials. 8,37,79,80,143,153,361 Compared with NEB calculations (section 4.2), which require a priori

guesses of migration pathways as input, MD simulations provide a visual of the dynamics and trajectory of ions (Figure 31a), which are directly observed to determine di /uniFB00 usion mechanisms. For example, AIMD simulations by Mo et al. indicated that LGPS was a 3D ion conductor with fast 1D conduction through the c -channels and slower cross-channel ab -planar di /uniFB00 usion, 37 contrary to the early speculation of 1D conduction in LGPS. 6 This prediction was con /uniFB01 rmed by later experiments, which detected Li-ion conduction in both the c and ab directions at room temperature. 335,398,399 By observing the Li-ion di /uniFB00 usion dynamics using AIMD simulations, highly correlated di /uniFB00 usion mechanisms such as concerted migration of multiple ions in the disordered Li sublattice of Li superionic conductor materials, such as LGPS, garnet-structured LLZO, and NASICON LATP, have been revealed. 87,102,396,400,401

The di /uniFB00 usional events of ions that occur during the time interval of an MD simulation can be aggregated and analyzed to quantify the di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient and conductivity of mobile ions. The D * of an ion can be quanti /uniFB01 ed from the mean-squared displacement (MSD) of a given mobile ion over a set time interval Δ t :

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

where d is the dimension of the system. MSD can be given by the following equation:

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

where N is the total number of ions that are di /uniFB00 using and r i ( t ) is the displacement of the i th ion at time t . The evaluation of MSD should be performed over a statistically appropriate ensemble over many possible states of the systems, which are often approximated as a range of states with di /uniFB00 erent starting times with the interval Δ t . 402 In addition, the MSD can be evaluated and averaged for each individual ion, corresponding to the tracer di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient D * (section 2.1) at the time scale of MD simulation. If the MSD is evaluated for the center of mass of all mobile ions, the corresponding D is often called the jump di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient, which is physically similar to and is often regarded as the di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient D σ , corresponding the collective migration of many ions from macroscopic migration (section 2.1). The ratio between these two di /uniFB00 usivities from MD simulations is often used to estimate the Haven ratio in computation modeling. 80 These di /uniFB00 usional properties are evaluated in the same manner in kinetic Monte Carlo modeling. 403

The ionic conductivity is estimated from the di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient using the Nernst -Einstein relation (eq 4). Although eq 4 has been widely used to calculate ionic conductivity, the use of the tracer di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient D * estimated from individual ion migration would often lead to an underestimation of the ionic conductivity because of the strong correlation of ion migration in fast ion-conductor materials. 80,402,404

Similar to the impedance measurements performed at di /uniFB00 erent temperatures, the ionic conductivity σ of a material can be determined from multiple AIMD simulations at di /uniFB00 erent temperatures. The calculated conductivities yield the following Arrhenius relationship:

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

By /uniFB01 tting log( σ T ) vs 1/ T , the activation energy E a and the prefactor σ 0 can be determined. The /uniFB01 tted Arrhenius relation can be used to estimate the ionic conductivity at other temperatures through extrapolation, assuming the di /uniFB00 usion mechanisms are identical at the extrapolated temperatures. Given that many AIMD simulations are performed at temperatures much higher than 300 K (often greater than 600 -1200 K) with a small number of atoms (e.g., a few hundred in total), such an extrapolation may not be quantitatively accurate or physically valid for some materials.

In addition, because the estimation of di /uniFB00 usion properties from MD simulations is based on the statistics of many di /uniFB00 usional events, an appropriate estimation of statistical variance and error bounds is essential. For AIMD simulations, which have a limited number of atoms and a short time duration on the order of a few hundred picoseconds, the total number of di /uniFB00 usional events is highly limited. He et al. systematically tested various parameters in the estimation of di /uniFB00 usional properties from AIMD simulations and proposed a procedure to minimize the errors during the quanti /uniFB01 cation of simulation results. 402 In addition, they proposed a scheme to quantify the statistical variances of di /uniFB00 usional properties, which are directly dependent on the total number of e /uniFB00 ective ion hops during the MD simulation. Therefore, the quantitative evaluation of di /uniFB00 usional properties using AIMD simulations should only be applicable to materials with fast ion conduction and at elevated temperatures. From their estimation for a typical fast ion conductor material, the di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient from an AIMD simulation may have a relative standard deviation of 20 -50%. Given that the error bounds of di /uniFB00 usional results from AIMD simulations are nonnegligible, an appropriate estimation of error bounds should always be performed and reported.

In addition to the aforementioned di /uniFB00 usional properties, MD simulations reveal additional atomistic information on ion dynamics that is not accessible from NEB calculations. For example, the spatial distribution of mobile-ion movements over time can be aggregated into the probability density of ion occupancy within the crystal structure. The ion probability density can be visualized to reveal di /uniFB00 usional pathways in the structure (Figure 31a). 37 In addition, the ion spatial distribution can be analyzed to determine the ion sites, such as the Li + sites in LGPS (Figure 31b). The unusual distribution of Li-ion sites in the LGPS structure has been noted as a key feature of superionic conductors, which exhibit concerted migration 80 and Li-ion sublattice frustration. 26

The time-evolution information on atoms from MD simulations can be used to estimate the van Hove correlation function to obtain further information on the di /uniFB00 usional mechanisms in a material. The distinct part of the van Hove function indicates the time correlation of the movement of one or more adjacent ions. The van Hove function calculated using AIMD simulations of Li superionic conductors, such as LGPS (Figure 31c), LLZO, and NASICON, indicates that a vacated site becomes occupied within less than 1 ps, which suggests a strong time correlation between consecutive Li + migration events. 80 Such closely correlated hopping of ions is a result of the concerted migration of multiple ions, which has been observed to be a general di /uniFB00 usion mechanism in superionic conductors. 80,87,102,396 As illustrated in these examples, AIMD simulations are particularly advantageous in studying timedependent aspects of ion-migration mechanisms, which are less straightforward to assess using static computation methods including the NEB method.

Figure 32. Evaluation of phase stability. (a) Schematic illustration of an energy convex hull in the A -B composition space. The γ phase with an energy above the hull E hull is referenced to the combination of stable phases, i.e., phase equilibria. (b) Compositional substitution to create new Li10MP2X12 (M = Ge, Si, Sn; X = O, S) compounds in the LGPS structure (left) and the predicted E hull of new Li 10 MP2X12. Reproduced with permission from ref 361. Copyright 2018 Elsevier.

Because AIMD simulations rely on /uniFB01 rst principles, they can be used to predict novel superionic conductors. A classic example is the study by Ong et al. that aimed to develop a less expensive alternative to LGPS through chemical substitution of the rare and expensive element Ge in the structure. 153 Using /uniFB01 rstprinciples computation with phase stability evaluation (section 4.5), two new stable structures, Li 10SnP2S12 and Li10 SiP2 S12 , were computationally predicted and exhibited high ionic conductivities of 6 and 23 mS cm -1 at 300 K, respectively, as calculated using AIMD simulations (Figure 31d). These predicted structures were the /uniFB01 rst reported alternatives to LGPS composed of more economical and earth-abundant Sn and Si instead of Ge. Moreover, experimental measurements of these materials, synthesized after their prediction, validated the accuracy of the AIMD simulations. Bron et al. measured the room-temperature Li-ion conductivity of Li 10SnP2S12 to be 7 mS cm -1 in the crystalline grain, in excellent agreement with AIMD simulations. 253 By incorporating Cl as a dopant in Li 10 SiP2 S12 , Kato et al. synthesized the superionic conductor Li 9.54 Si 1.74 P1.44 S11.7 Cl0.3 and measured the conductivity to be 25 mS cm -1 at room temperature, 3 which agrees well with the predicted conductivity from computation. Several groups have synthesized other fast-ion conductors in the Li -Si -P-S compositional family, including glass ceramic Li 7SiPS8 and Li 11 Si 2 PS12, with expected room temperature bulk conductivities of approximately 2 mS cm -1 and 7 mS cm -1 , respectively. 178,405 Using AIMD simulations, Richards et al. predicted that the Naion conductor Na10SnP2S12 with the LGPS structure exhibits a room-temperature Na + conductivity of 0.94 mS cm -1 with an activation energy of 0.32 eV. 8 Experimentally synthesized Na10SnP2S12 exhibited a conductivity of 0.4 mS cm -1 at room temperature and an activation energy of 0.356 eV, in excellent agreement with the AIMD simulations. 8 In addition to these selected examples, MD simulations have been successfully used to study and design new Li-ion conductors, 143,291,406 -408 oxideion conductors, 386 hydride-ion conductors, 409 and sodium-ion conductors. 362,410

## 4.4. Materials Stability

Thermodynamic databases and modeling programs are wellestablished tools to guide materials design and selection. 411 -413 Here, we summarize recent advancements in /uniFB01 rst-principles computation for evaluating materials stability. Evaluation of the phase stability of computationally predicted materials is an essential step for continuing further experimental and theoretical studies. 359 -362,414 -416 For a given materials system, a thermodynamic convex hull (Figure 32a) is constructed by comparing the energies of all compounds in the compositional space. Using this convex hull, the phase stability of a given material with composition C can be quanti /uniFB01 ed as the di /uniFB00 erence between its energy E (phase) to the minimum energy of the convex hull E eq (C) at the same composition, which is often a linear combination of ground-state phases on the convex hull. This energy di /uniFB00 erence, E hull (phase), is often called the ' energy above the hull. ' Speci /uniFB01 cally,

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

For a thermodynamically stable phase, the value of E hull would be zero. For a material with a positive value of E hull (e.g., composition γ in Figure 32a), there is an energetically favorable reaction for the decomposition of the phase into a set of phase equilibria on the convex hull, and the decomposition energy is the negative of E hull . Therefore, the value of E hull represents the thermodynamic driving force for the phase decomposition and can serve as a representation of the phase stability.

Recently, the evaluation of a material ' s phase stability using /uniFB01 rst-principles calculations has been facilitated by databases of theoretical thermodynamic materials data, such as the Materials Project (MP), 415 Open Quantum Materials Database (OQMD), 41 and Automatic Flow (AFLOW). 416 E hull of a given new material can be determined from DFT calculation of the energy convex hull and phase equilibria directly queried from the materials database. 153,361 This computational approach has been widely adopted in the prediction, design, and discovery of new materials. In the study by Ong et al., the authors evaluated the phase stability of structures in the Li10MP2X12 (M = Ge, Si, Sn; X = O, S) family of materials to /uniFB01 nd less expensive and moisture-stable alternatives to LGPS. 153 The oxides with O-to-S anion substitutions, Li10MP2O12 (M = Ge, Si, Sn), were calculated to have a high E hull greater than 90 meV/atom (Figure 32b), suggesting poor phase stability and synthesizability. In contrast, the Li 10 MP2S12 (M = Ge, Si, Sn) sul /uniFB01 des had relatively low E hull values of 25 meV/atom or less, suggesting good phase stability (Figure 32b). Li 10 SnP2S12, Li 10 SiP2 S12 , and its variant composition Li 10+ x Si 1+ x P2 -x S12 composition have since been successfully synthesized experimentally. 3,178,253,417,418 This approach has been applied to discover and predict an extensive variety of novel ion conductors,

including Li-ion conductors 406,407,419 and novel electrodes for Li-ion batteries, 420,421 oxide-ion conductors, 79 hydride-ion conductors, 409 magnesium-ion conductors, 7 and sodium-ion conductors. 362,422

Currently, the aforementioned evaluation of E hull uses mostly DFT energies from static calculations at 0 K. This is a fair approximation given the di /uniFB00 erences in the PV and TS terms are often small in solid -solid reactions. Monte Carlo modeling based on cluster expansion methods can account for the con /uniFB01 gurational contribution to entropy in a lattice model at elevated temperatures and has been applied to determine the energetics of atomic con /uniFB01 guration (e.g., the lowest energy orderings of a phase) and /uniFB01 nite-temperature properties, such as defect solubility limits, voltage pro /uniFB01 les, or phase diagrams. 363,407,423 -426 In addition, phonon calculations are also widely used to evaluate the vibrational contribution to the free energies. Richards et al. used Monte Carlo modeling based on cluster expansion methods in combination with phonon calculations to produce a /uniFB01 nite-temperature phase diagram for the solubility of Zn in Li 1+2 x Zn1 -x PS4, a newly predicted solid electrolyte. 407 This diagram provided an indication of the experimental feasibility of synthesizing a structure with high Li concentration and high ionic conductivity. Since this work, Li 1+2 x Zn1 -x PS4 has been synthesized and demonstrated successfully as a solid electrolyte for an all-solid-state battery, con /uniFB01 rming the computational prediction results. 427

The limitations of the phase stability calculations should be noted as follows. In addition to the assumption of using static DFT energies at 0 K and the lack of contribution from the TS and PV terms, the accuracy of the phase stability calculation is also a /uniFB00 ected by the accuracy of the DFT energies of all phases in the system; 428 therefore, care must be taken for certain types of materials for which the DFT energy may be inaccurate. Fitting regimes using DFT and DFT+U energies to experimental energies were employed to improve the accuracy in the prediction of formation energies of transition metal oxides and intercalation voltages of conversion electrodes for Li-ion batteries. 429,430 In addition, typical DFT calculations often neglect the e /uniFB00 ects of van der Waals interactions, which are more common in organic molecular compounds or layered materials. 431 -433 The use of DFT functionals including van der Waals interactions or even correction terms results in signi /uniFB01 cant improvements in reproducing the formation energies of layered and molecular materials, 431,432,434 and their use should be considered to improve the accuracy of DFT energies and phase stability predictions. Second, the phase stability evaluation requires the DFT energy of all compounds in the relevant chemical space. 374 In a less-well studied or high-dimension system for which some low-energy compounds are unknown or not included, the evaluation may lead to false-positive predictions of stable phases.

Even with these limitations of DFT calculations, the metastability as assessed using E hull may not be su /uniFB03 cient ascertain the synthesizability of a material but rather is e /uniFB00 ective for ruling out materials with poor synthesizability. Sun et al. summarized the phase stability E hull values for a wide range of materials and proposed that the stability of materials at synthesis conditions plays a central role in the formation of the materials. 435 Their analyses identi /uniFB01 ed many hypothetical polymorphs of well-explored binary oxides with low E hull , but they argued these hypothetical polymorphs were not likely to be synthesized. Therefore, low E hull is not a su /uniFB03 cient criterion for synthesizability and this may explain why some predicted materials with low E hull are di /uniFB03 cult to synthesize. In addition, they proposed that the stability of the intermediate phases under synthesis conditions is key to the formation of the metastable phases. This theory has been supported by many recent theoretical and experimental studies. 436 -441

## 4.5. Comparisons and Limitations of Computational Techniques

As reviewed above, the computational techniques for studying di /uniFB00 usional properties, such as the NEB method, defect calculations, and MD simulations, are highly complementary for understanding various aspects of di /uniFB00 usional mechanisms. The NEB method enables quanti /uniFB01 cation of the migration energy pro /uniFB01 le and barrier for given ion-di /uniFB00 usion pathways within a structure. The di /uniFB00 usional pathway and mobile carriers (e.g., vacancy, interstitial, interstitialcy, or multi-ion complex) are preassigned as input for a NEB calculation. To obtain a full picture of the di /uniFB00 usion mechanism in a material, a range of potential mobile carriers and their formation energies and concentrations should be evaluated and compared (section 4.1) to determine the dominant mobile carrier for di /uniFB00 usion. For the dominant carriers, their corresponding migration pathways (section 4.2) should be comprehensively considered, especially in materials with complex crystal structures. In some materials such as superionic conductors with disordered mobile ion sublattices, a priori guesses of the carriers and their pathways may be challenging.

MD simulations (section 4.3) can greatly complement NEB methods for studying di /uniFB00 usion mechanisms, especially in materials with structural disordering. In MD simulations, the ion hopping is thermally activated, and viable migration pathways will be sampled because of thermal activation. Therefore, MD simulations can be used to model ion di /uniFB00 usion without preassigned di /uniFB00 usional pathways in contrast to the NEB method. MD simulations are particularly advantageous in the study of superionic conductors with a disordered mobile ion sublattice, in which the possible mechanisms of ion migration would be highly diverse and di /uniFB03 cult to guess a priori. For example, recent AIMD simulation studies revealed the concerted migration mechanism of multiple Li ions in Li superionic conductors. 26,80,87,102,396 Such migration modes observed from AIMD simulations were further evaluated by NEB methods for detailed energy pro /uniFB01 les during multi-ion concerted migration to illustrate the di /uniFB00 usional mechanisms. 80,87,102,396

In addition, MD simulations provide di /uniFB00 usional properties and mechanistic information that are not available from other methods because only MD simulations can directly model ion dynamics and di /uniFB00 usional events in real time. The timedependent positions of ions from MD simulations can be used to quantify a large amount of di /uniFB00 usional information, including the jumping rate, site occupancy, spatial probability density of mobile ions, correlation function, and radial distribution functions, 37,80,387,388,402,408 which are crucial atomic-scale details for understanding di /uniFB00 usion mechanisms. In addition, MD simulations can be used to determine the conductivity and di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient at di /uniFB00 erent temperatures, which obey an Arrhenius relationship. This Arrhenius relationship can be used to calculate the activation energy and the prefactor, which comprehensively account for the physical statistical sampling of various ion migration modes in a material. In addition to the activation energy, the importance of the prefactor has been emphasized in a few recent studies. Zeier and co-workers

stressed the coupling between the prefactor and activation energy, also known as the Meyer -Nedel rule, 442 in their study of the conductivity of argyrodites Li6PS5X (X = Cl, Br, I) with di /uniFB00 erent halide anions. 31 They suggested a decrease in the activation energy by increasing the polarizability of the anion sublattice would also decrease the prefactor, leading to only a marginal increase in the conductivity. Di Stefano et al. argued the record room temperature Li + di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient in the new superionic conductor LiTi2(PS4)3 was achieved because of its increased prefactor. 26,443 Therefore, a single parameter, such as the migration barrier or activation energy, is insu /uniFB03 cient for describing the fast ion conduction of a given ion conductor. Among the computational techniques reviewed here, MD simulation is well suited to provide a full set of these di /uniFB00 usional properties including the prefactor in addition to visualization of atomic-scale dynamics.

AIMD simulations have been widely adopted to study ion conductor materials thanks to their capability to be readily applied to new materials with novel structures and chemistries. In contrast, the use of classical MD simulations is often limited by the available choices of interatomic potentials or force /uniFB01 elds. However, AIMD simulations cannot be applied to analyze di /uniFB00 usion in every scenario and are often limited to fast ion conductors at elevated temperatures. 402 AIMD simulations require a signi /uniFB01 cant computational cost and are thus limited to a short physical time (on the order of 100 ps to 1 ns) and a small system size (on the order of a few hundred atoms). However, the accuracy of AIMD simulations in quantifying di /uniFB00 usional properties relies on the comprehensive sampling of a large number of di /uniFB00 usional events observed during the simulation time. Thus, AIMD simulation is generally unsuitable for studying materials with low conductivity and large activation energies because an insu /uniFB03 cient number of ion hops observed during the simulation would lead to erroneous di /uniFB00 usional properties. For the same reason, AIMD simulation is often performed at high temperatures (often 600 K and higher) to increase the number of di /uniFB00 usional events observed. Discrepancies may also arise in cases where the di /uniFB00 usion mechanism at high temperatures is di /uniFB00 erent from that at the temperature of physical interest, for example, in materials that undergo a phase transition or superionic transition between these temperature ranges. Finally, because the estimation of di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient and MSD is a statistical averaging of the stochastic di /uniFB00 usional events that occur during the AIMD simulation, the estimated values from AIMD simulations are subject to statistical variance and error (discussed in section 4.3). 402 Kinetic Monte Carlo method may be an alternative approach to overcome some limitations of AIMD simulations, if a list of fundamental hopping mechanisms can be determined for the materials of interest. 444 -447

Further, AIMD simulations, along with the majority of the atomistic computational methods reviewed here, are most commonly conducted for bulk crystalline structures and are only representative of the di /uniFB00 usion and conductivity in the bulk crystalline phase. A few classical MD simulations have been performed for grain boundaries or amorphous materials. 448 -450 Experimental measurements of the conductivity in polycrystalline materials usually include the bulk and grain-boundary conductivity and any compositional variations or second-phase particles arising from these synthesis conditions, which may be another reason for discrepancy with experimentally determined conductivity values.

## 5. DISCUSSION

## 5.1. Combination of Computational and Experimental Techniques

By combining the strengths of computational and experimental methods, detailed processes of ion transport can be revealed. 208 Taking the heavily studied garnet-structure Li-ion conductor Li7La3Zr2O12 as an example, traditional powder di /uniFB00 raction combined with the maximum entropy method (MEM) enable depiction of the ion transport process. A continuous 3D moving channel is revealed, and lithium ions migrate in the structure via the 24 d -96 g -48 g -96 h -24 d path, in which the tetrahedral, centered octahedral, and o /uniFB00 -center octahedral sites are all involved. These di /uniFB00 usion phenomena have been further supported by computational studies, 87,396 leading to a concerted lithium-ion transport mechanism similar to the MEM results. In addition to the lithium migration pathways, the activation barrier and Li ionic conductivity were also provided by the computational results. The calculated overall activation barrier for Li di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient from MD simulations in LLZO is 0.33 eV, which is in excellent agreement to the experimental value of 0.30 -0.34 eV. The transport dynamics of lithium in the garnet structure was experimentally con /uniFB01 rmed using 7 Li spin -lattice relaxation NMR spectroscopy. 87,195,451 The jump frequency of lithium was calculated to be 1.9 × 10 5 s -1 at 325 K and 4.9 × 10 8 s -1 at 530 K. In addition, β is 1.7 (smaller than 2), which con /uniFB01 rmed that lithium migration in LLZO is highly correlated.

For the thiophosphate Li 10GeP2S12, the dimensionality of lithium transport was under debate (1D vs 3D). On the basis of synchrotron XRD and neutron di /uniFB00 raction patterns, a 1D lithium-ion conductive channel along the c -axis with an activation barrier of 0.25 eV was determined. 6 This was supported by a follow-up AIMD simulation study, indicating that Li-ion di /uniFB00 usion in 1D channels happens in a string-like fashion. 102 However, MD simulation suggested an anisotropic 3D lithium ionic conduction network: the di /uniFB00 usion channel along the c -axis with activation energy of 0.17 eV, but in the ab plane perpendicular to the c -axis with a higher activation energy of 0.28 eV. 37 Adams et al. proposed a new Li4 (Wycko /uniFB00 4 c ) site, and they believed that the lithium-ion migration in the plane was achieved through the Li4 site located between the channels, which avoids 1D channel blockage, enables 3D lithium-ion di /uniFB00 usion and thus generates excellent ionic conductivity in LGPS. 38 The existence of the Li4 site was later con /uniFB01 rmed by SCXRD of LGPS, followed by con /uniFB01 rmation of lithium motion in a 3D network with comparable activation energy in the ab plane and Li1, Li3 (16 h ) sites, and along the c -axis through Li1 and Li3(16 h ) and Li4 (4 c ) sites. 399 Currently, it is more generally accepted that lithium ions move via a 3D pathway in LGPS rather than a 1D pathway.

Perovskite-type yttrium-doped BaXO3 (X = Zr, Ce) is one group of the most widely studied proton conductors, 452 and various ion transport mechanisms have been proposed based on the /uniFB01 ndings of experimental or computational techniques. The formation of fast proton conduction paths by local lattice softening and the entropic stabilization of proton defects allows BaXO3-based materials to exhibit high proton conductivity. 453,454 The comparison between QENS and DFT results suggests that the movement of hydrogen ions is attributed to proton transfer in octahedra near dopants. 455 QENS and impedance measurements con /uniFB01 rm that the cerium -oxygen and hydroxyl stretching modes propel the protons as charge carriers. 456 By combining EIS, thermogravimetric, and NMR

analysis, Yamazaki et al. demonstrated that in Y-doped BaZrO 3 protons are trapped by the YO6 octahedra and must overcome the association energy of 29 kJ mol -1 between protons and dopants to become trap-free, then move with the migration energy of 16 kJ mol -1 to complete long-range transport. 46 Neutron and X-ray di /uniFB00 raction reveal that protons follow a superstructure organized by yttrium. 286 DFTand kinetic Monte Carlo simulations show that ordered dopant structures can strongly enhance proton conductivity. 287

## 5.2. Classi /uniFB01 cation of Di /uniFB00 usion Coe /uniFB03 cients

Di /uniFB00 usion in crystalline solids occurs via a series of jumps of individual atoms or a group of atoms. The di /uniFB00 usion in a lattice can be expressed by physical quantities that describe the fundamental jump process: the jump distance and jump frequency. Two basic parameters are relevant to ion transport, the di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient and mobility. The D σ is mobile ion concentration dependent, whereas mobility is not. Self-di /uniFB00 usion is the random translational motion of atoms and is driven by internal kinetic energy in an equilibrium state without external driving force (e.g., electrical /uniFB01 eld). In the study of solid-state ionic conductors, many techniques, including variable-temperature NMR, NMR relaxometry, 2D NMR, QENS, μ + SR, PFG NMRandtracer di /uniFB00 usion, applies no external driving force, thus, the di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cients obtained from these ways are considered the self-di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient. In this regard, EIS measurement is an exception.

It is worth noting that di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cients determined using almost all characterization techniques are averaged, which should, however, in principle vary across di /uniFB00 erent length and time scales. Below, we illustrate the di /uniFB00 erences between di /uniFB00 erent di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cients. Taking the garnet-structured Li-ion conductor Li7La3Zr2O12 as an example, the lithium ion jump processes from ps to μ s probed using di /uniFB00 erent techniques are presented in Figure 33. Because of technical limitations, only a certain range of jump frequencies can be probed; however, this does not mean that there is only one jump frequency during the process of ion migration. As shown in Figure 33a, during the entire ion transport process, at least three ionic conduction processes are present with di /uniFB00 erent jump frequencies, ranging from kHz to GHz. Moreover, the height of the activation barrier was observed to be negatively correlated to the jump frequency (Figure 33b). For μ + SR, only the very local ionic jump is probed and may not interact with other grains or grain boundaries; therefore, ions only experience a relatively small activation barrier to hop to adjacent sites. In contrast, EIS is sensitive to long-range ion transport, where the grain boundary e /uniFB00 ect is very often present and can lead to a higher activation barrier.

## 5.3. Determination of Activation Energy

The activation barrier can be calculated from the temperaturedependent ionic conductivity measured by EIS. However, we emphasize that the extrapolated activation barriers based on the Arrhenius relationship are di /uniFB00 erent when plotting ln( σ ) vs 1/ T or ln( σ T ) vs 1/ T . For clari /uniFB01 cation, we take the natural logarithm of eq 25 and rearrange it as the following:

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

Therefore, to calculate the activation barrier, ln( σ T ) should be plotted against 1/ T . As shown in Figure 34, even in a relatively narrow temperature range, the activation barrier di /uniFB00 ers substantially depending on whether temperature is taken into account in the conductivity term.

Figure 33. (a) Schematic illustration of lithium transport process in Li7La3Zr2O12 with di /uniFB00 erent di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient. The heights of the activation barriers are not scaled. The ions in red, green, and blue indicate the motion of ions that can be probed by μ + SR, NMR relaxometry, and EIS measurements, respectively. (b) Di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cients and activation barriers of Li in Li7La3Zr2O12 using various techniques. The squares and ( × ) symbols represent the di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient and activation barrier, respectively. The symbols in red, green, and blue were probed using NMR relaxometry, μ + SR, and EIS measurements, respectively. All the di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient data were measured at room temperature. Data of activation barrier and di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cients are obtained from refs 195,232,270, and 457.

Figure 34. Arrhenius conductivity plot of Li10GeP2S12 (circles) and Li 1.5 Al0.5Ge1.5P3O12 (squares). The conductivity was measured from 258 to 298 K for LGPS and from 241 to 293 K for LAGP. Di /uniFB00 erences of 0.024 and 0.022 eV were observed in LGPS and LAGP when ln( σ ) or ln( σ T) was plotted against 1/ T , respectively.

As we discussed earlier in section 2, this extrapolated activation energy contains information on the defect formation energy for intrinsic defect driven ion migration processes and possibly contains information regarding the defect trapping energy for extrinsic defect driven ion transport processes. We further note that the activation energy deduced from EIS measurement may not be directly comparable to that from MD simulation because they are not on the same time and length scale. This e /uniFB00 ect is also demonstrated in Figure 33; the activation energies probed by di /uniFB00 erent techniques, which are sensitive to di /uniFB00 erent time and length scales, di /uniFB00 er greatly. For the proton conductor Ca-doped LaPO4, 458 QENS measurements revealed an activation barrier of 0.1 eV, whereas a higher value of 1 eV was obtained by EIS measurements. It has been suggested that QENS probes the oscillatory jump of ions between adjacent tetrahedra, whereas EIS probes long-range intertetrahedral di /uniFB00 usion, resulting in di /uniFB00 erent activation barriers corresponding to di /uniFB00 erent ion transport processes.

## 5.4. Estimation of Mobile Ion Concentration

It remains challenging to obtain the exact value of the mobile ion concentration. This has been highlighted in the work by Roling and his co-workers, 459 which suggest that mobile ion concentration should be only discussed when referring to a particular time scale. While considering extremely long time scales (days or years), all carrier ions (e.g., Li + in Li conductors) must be mobile ions; at extremely short time scale, many ions are trapped at equilibrium positions, and as a result, mobile ion concentration must be much smaller than that of carrier ion concentration. Yamazaki et al. elaborated the coexistence of trapped and trap-free protons in Y-doped BaZrO3 proton conductor. 46

By using complementary techniques, di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cients at di /uniFB00 erent time scales can be determined. Combining the Nernst -Einstein relation (eq 4) and = * σ H D D R , mobile ion concentration can be estimated by eq 28.

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

As discussed in section 2.1, for most ionic conductors, the Haven ratio H R is less than or equal to 1. Because the ionic conductivity σ and tracer di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient D * can be directly measured by

EIS and PFG NMR, the maximum mobile ion concentration can be estimated assuming H R equals to 1, which means an uncorrelated transport process. In Table 4, we present some mobile ion concentration of lithium ionic conductors calculated by eq 28, with H R being equal to 1, and compare with their theoretical ion concentration. It can be found that for some ionic conductors, such as Li3N ( c -axis) and Li 7 La3Zr2O12, the mobile ion concentrations were smaller than those estimated from the crystallography parameter (mostly on the order of 10 28 m -3 ). Therefore, care must be taken when estimating the mobile ion concentration on the basis of the crystal structure only. Other techniques also suggested that the assumption of all carrier atoms being mobile is inappropriate. For example, Mori et al. 200 observed a broad peak near the elastic incoherent scattering peak in Li 7 P3S11 , which indicated that not all the Li ions were mobile. Yoshida et al. 460 observed a broad Gaussian peak in 1 H NMR measurements of single-crystal CsHSO4, suggesting that immobile protons were present. On the other hand, the calculated mobile ion concentrations are larger than those estimated from chemical compositions for thio-LISICONs, such as Li 7 GePS8, Li10 GeP2S12, and Li 10 SnP2S12. This suggests that in thio-LISICONs, the Haven ratio HR should be smaller than 1, indicating the strong ion correlations, consistent with current understanding of correlated ion transport in these materials. 80,102

In addition, we also observed that the mobile ion concentration is not always a constant but can be temperature dependent, which in some cases, e.g., in Li7La3Zr2O12 (LLZO), follows approximately an Arrhenius relationship, as observed in Figure 35. This temperature-dependent behavior of the mobile ion concentration resembles that of defect concentrations, indicating that defects and carriers are most likely correlated in LLZO. We further calculated the slope through linear /uniFB01 tting, which corresponds to an activation energy of 3.88 eV. Intriguingly, this extrapolated activation energy is close to the lithium vacancy formation energy in LLZO (3.6 eV) and is much lower than the lithium interstitial formation energy (5.8 eV), 461 suggesting that the ion transport process of LLZO is likely bene /uniFB01 ted from lithium vacancy formation.

## 5.5. Emerging Experimental Techniques

Using the various techniques developed to date, several physical parameters related to the ion transport process discussed in

Figure 35. Arrhenius plot of mobile ion concentration of LLZO vs 1000/ T . The mobile ion concentrations at di /uniFB00 erent temperatures were calculated from measured di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cients from PFG NMR of ref 463 and conductivity from impedance measurements of ref 290.

section 2, including the ionic conductivity, tracer di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient, and corresponding activation energy, can be reliably obtained.

For the jump distance and mobile ion concentration, approximations are often used. The jump distance is often estimated from crystallographic data, such as XRD and neutron di /uniFB00 raction. Alternatively, computational techniques can be used to identify the minimum-energy migration path and thus the jump distance at the same time. Unfortunately, even today, it remains di /uniFB03 cult to precisely measure the mobile ion concentration, which is often assumed to be the total concentration of carrier ions in crystal structure or the defect concentration. However, as discussed earlier, this assumption is most likely incorrect.

Hall e /uniFB00 ect measurements could be an e /uniFB00 ective way to measure the carrier ion concentration and mobility. In semiconductors, the Hall e /uniFB00 ect is often utilized to determine the carrier concentration (electrons and/or holes). When a /uniFB02 ux of charged particles passes through a conducting material in an exerted magnetic /uniFB01 eld, a potential di /uniFB00 erence is generated, expressed by μ μ = | | = | | = | | σ V bE H H H I d I H H H ned , where V H is the Hall voltage being measured, and μ H, b , d , E , H , σ , n , and e are the Hall mobility, sample width, sample thickness, electric /uniFB01 eld, magnetic /uniFB01 eld, conductivity, carrier concentration, and carrier charge, respectively. With the measured Hall voltage and all other known constants, the Hall mobility of the carrier and carrier concentration can be directly derived.

For ionic conductors, Hall e /uniFB00 ect measurements of several fast silver conductors were performed in early research to determine ion transport mechanisms. 465 -467 A study of C5H6NAg5I6 467 suggested the mobile Ag + ion concentrations to be around 5% of all Ag + in the compound, leading to the conclusion that the high conductivity of C5H6NAg5I6 originated from only a small number of mobile ions. Newman et al. 466 measured the Hall mobilities of C5H6NAg5I6, RbAg4I5, and α -AgI and attributed the apparent di /uniFB00 erence between the /uniFB01 rst two ( μ / μ Br ∼ 1.3 × 10 4 , 65) and α -AgI ( μ / μ Br ∼ 1) to their distinct ion transport mechanisms, i.e., a non-Brownian, correlated ' di /uniFB00 usion pipe ' mechanism and random Brownian mechanism, respectively. The ratio μ / μ Br represents the ratio between the sample Hall mobility to that of strictly Brownian motion and is hence indicative of the ion transport mechanism. Liou et al. 468,469 argued that strong correlation e /uniFB00 ects of mobile ions are prerequisites for an ionic Hall voltage to be generated in a crystal. According to previous works, the Hall e /uniFB00 ect may not be observable in ionic conductors associated with uncorrelated ion transport. For recently discovered ionic conductors with strong correlation e /uniFB00 ects during ion transport processes, however, ionic Hall e /uniFB00 ect measurement can be a powerful tool to precisely determine the mobile ion concentration and mobility in these materials.

Migration entropy and attempt frequency are two other physical parameters that are almost impossible to quantitatively measure experimentally. The entropy term is contributed by con /uniFB01 gurational and vibrational entropy, mostly determined by disorder and vibrational frequency of the lattice dynamics, respectively. For example, in Li-ion conductor LGPS the con /uniFB01 gurational entropy term is determined from the partially occupied Li sites and the local disorder of Ge and P.

One potential method to determine the entropy term is to evaluate from the /uniFB01 tting results of EIS. The values of resistance R and nonideal capacitor constant phase element C can be /uniFB01 tted choosing proper equivalent circuit. The parameters of constant phase element C can can be determined via C = Q 1/ n R (1 -n )/ n . Here, Q is a capacitance-like empirical parameter, and n is typically related to the distribution of capacitance values in dielectric structures or the correlation between moving ions. Therefore, the jump frequency ν can be estimated by ν = RC 1 . Taking the natural logarithm of eq 2 and plotting ln ν vs 1/ T , the entropy term can be extrapolated from the intercept assuming an approximated attempt frequency ( ν 0 ) on the order of 10 13 . However, an assumed attempt frequency is a prerequisite for the analysis; thus, the calculated entropy is only an approximation. The attempt frequency is generally considered to be proportional to but not equal to the Debye frequency. From earlier computational results, it appears that the attempt frequency of ion transport in superionic conductors can be as short as the ps level. Therefore, it is necessary to probe the attempt frequency using a technique that can achieve the required temporal resolution of ps. In this regard, the following two techniques can be applied to measure the attempt frequency of ion migration in superionic conductors.

Terahertz time domain spectroscopy (THz-TDS) is used to measure the time evolution of terahertz electrical /uniFB01 eld intensity after each terahertz pulse (that is after the interaction of the terahertz pulse with the sample). Fourier transformation can be performed on the time-domain data of terahertz electric /uniFB01 eld intensity vs t to obtain amplitude and phase information of the sample, which can be used to evaluate the THz absorption and conductivity properties. Because the excitation pulse width lies in the picosecond range, THz-TDS is a promising tool to probe ion transport processes in the picosecond range. 470,471 The THz technique has been utilized to investigate carrier dynamics in semiconductors. For instance, using the optical-pump terahertzprobe (OPTP) technique, noncontact photoconductivity measurements of gallium nitride nanowires (NWs) revealed a long conductivity life (&gt;2 ns) and high mobility (820 ± 120 cm 2 (V s) -1 ). 472

In addition to its wide application in characterizing electron transport processes, recently, terahertz spectroscopy has been successfully applied to characterize ion transport in solid electrolytes. 473 In yttria-stabilized zirconia Zr0.84 Y0.16O2 -δ (8YSZ), the basis jump step is considered to be oxygen ions moving to an adjacent unoccupied site (i.e., an oxygen vacancy). However, oxygen vacancies tend to be clustered, thereby

Figure 36. (a) Potential for oxygen vacancy near a dopant atom in an oxide-ion conductor. (b) Conductivity at low (blue) and high temperatures (red). (c) Arrhenius conductivity plot of 8YSZ. The red dots show the temperature dependence of the THz conductivity of 8YSZ at 0.36 THz. The blue dots indicate the low-frequency conductivity in 8YSZ. The solid lines are the /uniFB01 tting results using the Arrhenius equation. Reproduced with permission from ref 473. Copyright 2019 Springer Nature.

substantially a /uniFB00 ecting long-range ion transport. As a result, oxygen ion transport must overcome both the migration energy and binding energy, as indicated by the blue arrow in Figure 36a. The oxygen ion oscillation near the dopant atom (several THz) before the jump occurs can be detected using THz-TDS (yellow arrow in Figure 36a). If the oscillation becomes stronger, the conductivity under 1 THz becomes obvious at high temperature (red line in Figure 36b). As observed in Figure 36b, the yellow shadow area (frequency slightly lower than 1 THz) becomes large as the temperature increases, whereas the conductivity at low frequency barely changes. Therefore, compared with longrange impedance measurement, very di /uniFB00 erent and useful information can be obtained through THz-TDS. When conductivity is plotted vs temperature as shown in Figure 36c, the slope from the impedance measurement is obviously larger than that from the THz-TDS measurement. THz-TDS thus provides a useful way to investigate the ionic conduction process at the ps scale and can be used to separate the ion migration energy from the defect formation energy and defect trapping energy, which are often convoluted together with the migration energy from other measurement techniques. We believe that THz-TDS can be used to study lithium, sodium, and proton conductors, whose attempt frequencies also lie in the terahertz range. With further development of THz-TDS techniques, hidden mechanisms of ion transport that are not accessible by other techniques could be revealed.

Ultrafast optical techniques can be used to characterize the defect and impurity reactions, dissociation, and migration in solid-state materials on the picosecond time scale. These techniques give detailed signals about the rates and pathways of vibrational energy transfer channels as well as the vibrational lifetimes of the local modes. The most common time-resolved method for vibrational lifetime measurements is the pump -probe transient bleaching technique utilizing ultrashort pulses from a tunable infrared laser. These methods also provide new insights into proton transport in solid proton conductors at moderate temperatures. Measurements of the O -H and O -D vibrational lifetimes using a combination of picosecond transient bleaching technique and infrared absorption (IRA) indicate that the room-temperature proton di /uniFB00 usion rate in rutile TiO 2 can be enhanced by 9 orders of magnitude when stimulated by resonant infrared light. The lifetimes measured by picosecond transient bleaching agree well with the measured IR line width. 474 Experimental observation of excited state proton tunneling as a decay channel of the O -H vibrational mode in the perovskite proton-conducting oxide KTaO3 has been reported. The tunneling rate from the excited state was 7 orders of magnitude larger than that from the ground state in the proton conductor BaCeO3. 475

## 6. CONCLUSIONS AND FUTURE PERSPECTIVES

In this review, we explained in detail the various physical parameters involved in the ionic conduction process as well as the structural parameters that can a /uniFB00 ect these physical parameters. Several confusing aspects in the literature were also elaborated (the activation barrier, di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cients, mobile ion concentration, and computed stability of superionic conductors). We reviewed the characterization techniques with respect to ion transport mechanisms both in terms of experiments and computation. The advantages and disadvantages of these techniques were discussed. We analyzed the ion transport process at di /uniFB00 erent time and length scales, and it became apparent that the properties of ion transport (e.g., di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cients and activation barrier) at di /uniFB00 erent length and time scales can be dramatically di /uniFB00 erent in many solid electrolytes. Therefore, a combination of various techniques is required to characterize ion transport in solids.

Using existing techniques, several physical parameters related to ion transport can be accurately measured. However, some physical parameters, which are important for understanding ion transport behavior, have not been accurately measured or even measured at all. A few potential techniques to measure these physical parameters were proposed, such as THz-TDS spectroscopy and ionic Hall e /uniFB00 ect measurement.

Many challenges remain, and substantial room exists for further and deeper understanding of ion transport in crystalline solids including the following:

- (1) Defects are important for ion transport. The nature, location, concentration, and interaction of defects in ionic conductors can all a /uniFB00 ect ion transport to a great extent. How can we understand these aspects better both experimentally and computationally? Once a comprehensive understanding of defects in ionic conductors is developed, the crucial link between defects and carriers must also be established.
- (2) Recently, increasing attention has been focused on the relationship between lattice dynamics and ionic conduction, and it has been proposed that a softer lattice and lower phonon band center are correlated to high ionic conductivity. We believe the next milestone in this /uniFB01 eld is to identify the speci /uniFB01 c phonon modes that control ion transport; by doing so, rational strategies to design fast ionic conductors can be derived.
- (3) As the activation barrier and di /uniFB00 usion coe /uniFB03 cient of ion migration, among other transport-related physical parameters, are time-scale and length-scale dependent, techniques that can cover a wide range of time and length scales need to be combined to yield a relatively complete picture of ion transport processes in crystalline solids. To date, various techniques are available to probe macroscopic ion transport (EIS, PFG NMR, and tracer di /uniFB00 usion measurements) and microscopic ion transport (QENS, NMR, μ + SR, and AIMD). However, we also note that when ion-transport-related physical parameters are discussed, relevant time and length scales should be speci /uniFB01 ed, thus enabling ' apples-to-apples ' comparisons.
- (4) On the computational side, for the future study of di /uniFB00 usion properties, computational insights into the di /uniFB00 usion mechanism beyond that of bulk crystalline phases at larger length scales and longer time scales are highly desired. This requires the development of computational techniques with the versatility, accuracy, and accessibility of ab initio methods but at a fraction of the computational cost. Such modeling methods would be highly bene /uniFB01 cial and can be applied to signi /uniFB01 cantly extend the model size and time period of AIMD simulations and also enable the study of mesoscale materials features such as grain boundaries and interphase layers. 448,449 Recently, computational approaches using machine learning to extend the length scale have been attempted in ionconducting materials. 40,476 -478 Further development in computational modeling capabilities will be essential for improved understanding, computational design, and accelerated discovery of ion conducting materials.

Recently, we have witnessed the rapid development of advanced experimental and computational techniques, which go far beyond the length and time scales that were possible 20 years ago. We hope that the current review can motivate a rethink of the /uniFB01 eld and lead to innovative paths toward a comprehensive understanding of ion transport in crystalline fast ionic conductors.

She is currently a Ph.D. candidate at University of Michigan -Shanghai Jiao Tong University Joint Institute, Shanghai Jiao Tong University, supervised by Dr. Shou-Hang Bo. Her research is focused on the ionic transport mechanism of lithium superionic conductors.

Adelaide M. Nolan received her B.S. in Materials Science and Engineering from Cornell University in 2017. She is currently a doctoral candidate at the University of Maryland, College Park, in the Department of Materials Science and Engineering, under the supervision of Dr. Yifei Mo. Her research focuses on the computational modeling of solid electrolytes and electrodes for energy storage devices.

Peng Du received his B.S. at the School of Material Science and Engineering from Zhengzhou University, China, in 2017. Now he is a Ph.D. candidate under the supervision of Dr. Qianli Chen at University of Michigan -Shanghai Jiao Tong University Joint Institute of Shanghai Jiao Tong University, China. His current interests are related to proton conductor and thin /uniFB01 lms.

Yifan Wu received his M.Sc. degree in Materials Science and Engineering from Delft University of Technology, Netherlands, in 2017, and B.S. degree in Materials Science and Engineering from Southeast University, China, in 2015. Now he is working toward a Ph.D. degree in the University of Michigan -Shanghai Jiao Tong University Joint Institute, Shanghai Jiao Tong University, China. His research is focused on critical interfaces of lithium-ion and solid-state batteries.

Chao Yang received his B.S. degree in the Department of Materials Science and Engineering, Shandong University, in 2016 and M.S. degree at the University of Michigan -Shanghai Jiao Tong University Joint Institute, Shanghai Jiao Tong University, in 2019, supervised by Dr. Qianli Chen. His research interests are potential distribution and charge transport in perovskite solar cells.

Qianli Chen is Assistant Professor of Materials Science and Engineering at the University of Michigan -Shanghai Jiao Tong University Joint Institute. She holds a Dr.Sc. from ETH Zurich, a M.Sc. from KTH Royal Institute of Technology, and a B.Eng. from Southeast University. She was an Alexander von Humboldt postdoctoral research fellow at the MaxPlanck Institute for Polymer Research. Her research focuses on the charge carrier behavior in solids, including the transport and distribution of ions and electrons.

Yifei Mo is an Associate Professor of Materials Science and Engineering at the University of Maryland, College Park, USA. He received his Ph.D. from the University of Wisconsin -Madison and conducted his postdoctoral research at Massachusetts Institute of Technology. His research aims to advance the understanding, design, and discovery of engineering materials through computational techniques, with current emphases on critical materials problems in solid-state devices for energy storage and conversion.

Shou-Hang Bo received his B.S. in Chemistry from Fudan University in 2009 and his Ph.D. in Chemistry from Stony Brook University in 2014. Since 2014, Dr. Bo has been a postdoctoral fellow at the Department of Materials Science and Engineering, Massachusetts Institute of Technology, and Materials Sciences Division, Lawrence Berkeley National Laboratory. Dr. Bo joined the University of Michigan -Shanghai Jiao Tong University Joint Institute in 2017 as a tenure-track assistant professor. His recent research interests include solid-state electrolytes, interfaces, and imaging of solid-state batteries.

## ACKNOWLEDGMENTS

This work was supported by Shanghai Sailing Program (contract no. 18YF1411100) and the National Natural Science Foundation of China (grant no. 51802193).
